The remaining chapters of this part provide\ndetailed measurement\nand data analysis protocols for in-water radiometric\nprofiles (Chapter\ni0),\nthe Marine Optical Buoy (MOBY) radiometric\nobservatory\nfor vicarious calibration\nof satellite\nocean color sensors (Chapter\n11), above water measurements\nof remote sensing reflectance\n(Chapter\n12), determinations\nof exact\nnormalized\nwater-leaving\nradiance\n(Chapter\n13),\natmospheric\nradiometric\nmeasurements\nto determine\naerosol optical thickness and sky radiance\ndistributions\n(Chapter 14), determination\nof absorption\nspectra from water samples (Chapter\n15),\nand determination\nof phytoplankton\npigment\nconcentrations\nusing HPLC (Chapter\n16) and\nfluorometric\n(Chapter 17) methods.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nData Reporting and Archival:\nChapter 18 describes the methods and procedures for data archival,\ndata synthesis and merging, and quality control applicable\nto the SeaWiFS Bio-optical\nArchive\nand Storage System (SeaBASS),\nwhich is maintained\nto support ocean color validation\nfor the\nSeaWiFS,\nSIMBIOS\nand other cooperating\nsatellite sensor projects.\nCurrent SeaBASS\nfile\ncontent and formatting requirements\nare given in Appendix B.\nWhat is new in Revision 3 to the ocean optics protocol document, as compared to Revision 2 (Fargion\nand Mueller 2000).\nThe most obvious changes are the insertion of 3 new chapters into the document, and\nthe renumbering\nof the other chapters to accommodate\nthem. The new chapters are:\n1.\n\nChapter 2, Fundamental\nDefinitions,\nRelationships\nand Conventions,\nintroduces\nthe radiometric\nquantities,\ninherent optical properties,\nfundamental\nconcepts and terminology\nunderlying\nthe in\nsitu measurement\nand analysis protocols discussed throughout\nthe document.\nThe chapter also\ndiscusses\nthe scales adopted\nin these protocols\nfor such quantities\nas extraterrestrial\nsolar\nirradiance,\nand the absorption and scattering coefficients\nof pure water.\n2.\nChapter 11, MOBY, A Radiometric\nBuoy for Performance\nMonitoring\nand Vicarious Calibration\nof Satellite Ocean Color Sensors:\nMeasurement\nand Data Analysis Protocols,\ndocuments\nthe\nspecific measurement\nand data analysis protocols used in the operation of this critical radiometric\nobservatory.\nThe MOBY\nnormalized\nwater-leaving\nradiance\ntime series has provided\nthe\nprincipal, common basis for vicarious calibration of every satellite ocean color sensor in operation\nsince 1996.\n3.\nChapter 13, Normalized\nWater-Leaving\nRadiance and Remote Sensing Reflectance:\nBidirectional\nReflectance and Other Factors, develops the physical basis underlying the bidirectional\naspects of\nthe ocean\'s\nreflectance,\nand presents methods\nfor removing\nthis effect to determine\nexact\nnormalized\nwater-leaving\nradiance,\nthe only form of water-leaving\nradiance\nsuitable\nfor\ncomparisons\nbetween determinations\nbased on satellite and in situ measurements.\nAside from renumbering,\nseveral of the chapters carried over from Revision 2 have been revisited and\nsignificantly\nrevised, while others have been modified only slightly. The two chapters providing overviews\nof Instrument\nCharacteristics\n(Chapter 4) and Field Measurements\nand Data Analysis (Chapter 9) have\nbeen revised to reflect the changed content of those two major parts of the document.\nChapter 15, covering\n\noo.\n111\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For SatelLite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nprotocols\nfor laboratory\nspectrophotometric\ndeterminations\nof absorption\nby particles\nand dissolved\nmaterials in seawater samples, has been significantly\nrevised to condense the workshop results reported in\nthe Revision 2 version into more focused descriptions\nof measurement\nand analysis protocols;\nthe more\ndetailed workshop results and background\nin the original version of this chapter (as cited in the present\nversion) comprise the single case where material presented in Revision 2 is not completely\nsuperceded\nby\nthe present document.\nProtocols for HPLC measurements\nof concentrations\nof phytoplankton\npigments\n(Chapter\n16) and fluorometric\nmeasurements\nof chlorophyll\na concentration\n(Chapter\n17) have been\nsignificantly\nupdated and revised.\nProtocols\nfor characterization\nof radiometers\n(Chapter\n6) and for\ncalibration of, and measurements\nusing, sun photometers\nand sky radiance instruments\n(chapters\n7 and 14)\nhave been updated significantly,\nbut modestly,\nand modifications\nto the remaining\nchapters\nare all\nrelatively\n\nminor.\n\nAlthough the\noptics protocols,\nprogress, or have\nthat will be taken\n\npresent document represents another significant,\nincremental\nimprovement\nin the ocean\nthere are several protocols\nthat have either been overtaken\nby recent technological\nbeen otherwise identified as inadequate.\nSome of the deficiencies\nand corrective\nsteps\nin Revision 4, scheduled for completion in 2002, include:\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe present state of the art in instruments\nand methods for determining\nproperties\n(IOP) is described only via abstract-level\nsummaries\nin Chapters\nchapter will provide more complete and up-to-date IOP related protocols.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAnother new chapter will address methods for radiometric\nand bio-optical\nmeasurements\nfrom moored and drifting buoys. These methods have much in common with, but also differ\nin many important respects\nfrom, those implemented\nfor the highly specialized\nMOBY\nvicarious calibration observatory\n(Chapter 11).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRadiometric\n\nmeasurements\n\nfrom\n\naircraft\n\nprotocols, but detailed methods are nowhere\nin Revision 4 to rectify this omission.\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nare\n\ndiscussed\n\ndiscussed.\n\nat several\n\npoints\n\nA third new chapter\n\ninherent\n4 and 9.\n\nin the\n\noptical\nA new\n\npresent\n\nwill be included\n\nRecent\nadvances,\nat the National\nInstitute\nof Standards and Technology\n(NIST),\nin\nradiometric\nstandards, methods of calibration,\nand stray light characterization\nhave outdated\nmuch of the material in the current protocols for characterization\nof radiometers\n(Chapter 6).\nKey improvements\nrelate to the NIST 2000 detector based scale of spectral irradiance,\nand\nthe NIST Spectral Irradiance and Radiance responsivity\nCalibrations\nwith Uniform Sources\n(SIRCUS)\nfacility.\nAn important\ngoal for Revision\n4 is to update the characterization\nprotocols of Chapter 6 to reflect these state-of-the-art\nmethods.\n\nThis technical report is not meant as a substitute for scientific literature.\nInstead, it will provide a\nready and responsive\nvehicle for the multitude of technical reports issued by an operational\nProject.\nThe\ncontributions\nare published as submitted, after only minor editing to correct obvious grammatical\nor clerical\nerrors.\n\niv\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nTable of Contents\n\nCHAPTER\n\nand Author List\n\n11 ...........................................................................................................................................\n\n138\n\nMOBY, A RADIOMETRIC BUOY FOR PERFORMANCEMONITORING AND VICARIOUS CALIBRATION OF SATELLITE\nOCEAN COLOR SENSORS: MEASUREMENT AND DATA ANALYSIS PROTOCOLS\nDennis K. Clark t, Mark\n\nBroenkow\n\nNOAA\n\n2, Yong\n\nNational\n\nSung\n\n2, Mike\n\nFeinholz\n\n2, Stephanie\n\nKim 3, B. Carol Johnson 4, Steven\nand James L. Mueller s\n\nSystems\n\n4National\nInstitute\nfor Hydro-Optics\n\nTechnologies,\n\nof Standards\nand Remote\n\nInc., RockvilIe,\n\nFlora 2, William\n\nW. Brown 4, Madlyn\n\nEnvironmental\nSatellite Data Information\nService,\n2Moss Landing Marine Laboratory,\nCalifornia\nJData\n\n5Center\n\nA. Yarbrough\n\nSuitIand,\n\nYuen I,\n\nMaryland\n\nMaryland\n\nand Technology,\nGaithersburg,\nMaryland\nSensing, San Diego State University,\nCalifornia\n\n11.1 INTRODUCTION\nThe Marine Optical Buoy (MOBY)\n(Clark et al. 1997) is the centerpiece\nof the primary ocean\nmeasurement\nsite for calibration\nof satellite\nocean color sensors\nbased on independent\nin situ\nmeasurements.\nSince late 1996, the time series of normalized\nwater-leaving\nradiances LwN(_,) determined\nfrom the array of radiometric\nsensors attached to MOBY are the primary basis for the on-orbit calibrations\nof the USA Sea-viewing\nWide Field-of-view\nSensor (SeaWiFS),\nthe Japanese\nOcean\nColor\nand\nTemperature\nSensor (OCTS), the French Polarization\nDetection Environmental\nRadiometer\n(POLDER),\nthe\nGerman Modular\nOptoelectronic\nScanner on the Indian Research Satellite 0RS1-MOS),\nand the USA\nModerate Resolution Imaging Spectrometer\n(MODIS).\nThe MOBY vicarious calibration Lwr_(g) reference\nis an essential dement in the international\neffort to develop a global, multi-year time series of consistently\ncalibrated ocean color products using data from a wide variety of independent\nsatellite sensors.\nA longstanding\ngoal of the SeaWiFS and MODIS (Ocean) Science Teams is to determine\nsatellitederived LwN(g) with a relative combined standard uncertainty I of 5 % (Chapter\n1). Other satellite ocean\ncolor projects and the Sensor Intercomparison\nfor Marine Biology and Interdisciplinary\nOceanic Studies\n(SIMBIOS)\nproject have also adopted this goal, at least implicitly.\nBecause water-leaving\nradiance\ncontributes at most 10 % of the total radiance measured by a satellite sensor above the atmosphere\n(Gordon\n1997), a 5 % uncertainty\nin LwN(g) implies a 0.5 % uncertainty\nin the above-atmosphere\nradiance\nmeasurements.\nThis level of uncertainty can only be approached\nusing "vicarious-calibration"\napproaches\nas described below. In practice, this means that the satellite radiance responsivity is adjusted to achieve the\nbest agreement,\nin a least-squares\nsense, for the LwN(_,) results determined\nusing the satellite and the\nindependent\noptical sensors (e.g. MOBY).\nThe end result of this approach\nis to implicitly\nabsorb\nunquantified,\nbut systematic,\nerrors in the atmospheric\ncorrection, incident solar flux, and satellite sensor\ncalibration\ninto a single correction\nfactor to produce consistency\nwith the in situ data (see e.g. Gordon\n1981, 1987, 1988).\nClearly, the combined standard uncertainty\nof the in situ LwN(Z) determinations\nmust be less than 5 %\nif the stated uncertainty\ngoal is to be approached.\nThe uncertainty budget of MOBY LwN(Jt) determinations\nmay be divided into environmental\nand radiometric\nfactors.\nEnvironmental\nfactors include uncertainties\n\nAll uncertainties\n\nin this document\n\nuncertainty\nis the uncertainty\nKuyatt 1994).\n\nare standard\n\nuncertainties,\n\nof the result of a measurement\n\n138\n\nunless noted otherwise.\nexpressed\n\nas a standard\n\nStandard\ndeviation\n\n(Taylor\n\nand\n\nOcean Protocols SateUite Ocean\nOptics\nFor\n\nColor Sensor Validation\n\ndue to radiance and irradiance fluctuations associated with surface waves and platform motions during the\nradiometric\nmeasurements,\nand with extrapolation\nof upwelling radiance measurements\nfrom depths of 1 m\nor more to, and through, the sea surface. The uncertainties\nassociated with these ambient conditions have\nbeen shown to be less than, but approaching,\n5 % for upwelled radiance (Siegel et al. , 1995; Hooker and\nMaritorena,\n2000).\nRadiometric\nuncertainty\ncomponents\nassociated\nwith instrument\ncharacterization,\ncalibration\nand stability, i.e. the radiance measurements\nper se, must be summed in quadrature to yield the\ncombined standard uncertainty of the MOBY Lws(2) determinations.\nThe estimated\n\ncombined\n\nstandard\n\nuncertainty\n\nof MOBY\n\nradiance\n\nmeasurements\n\nis between\n\n4 % and\n\n8 % (Clark et at 2001).\nThis estimate is based on uncertainties\nof MOBY calibrations at less than 3 %,\nchanges in pre- and post-deployment\ncalibrations\nranging from 1% to 6 %, radiometric\nstability tests\nduring deployments\nusing internal reference sources that show changes less than 1%, and diver-deployed\nexternal reference lamp responses that are stable within less than 3 % (the estimated\nuncertainty of the\nmethod) (Clark et al. 200i).\nThe 8 % upper limit on the combined standard uncertainty\nestimate does not\ninclude preliminary\nresults of recently undertaken\nstray light characterization\nof the MOBY spectrographs,\nwhich indicate systematic\nstray light offsets in LwN(_L) may have approximate\nmagnitudes\nof +5 % and\n-3 % at blue and green wavelengths\nrespectively\n(Sects. 11.4 and 11.8 below, and Clark et al. 2001). Once\nthe stray light characterization\nis completed\non all MOBY spectrographs,\nthe entire MOBY LwN(f).) time\nseries will be reprocessed\nwith an expected combined standard uncertainty\nof less than 5 %. Variations in\nthe measurement\nenvironment\nmay add additional uncertainty.\nThe nature of, and data requirements\nfor, vicarious calibration of a satellite ocean color sensor are\nbriefly described in Chapter 1 (Sect. 1.5), and in more detail by Gordon (1981, 1987, 1988, 1997), Gordon\net al. (1983), Evans and Gordon (1994), and Clark et al. (1997). A critical element of the procedure\nis the\nability to monitor a satellite sensor\'s performance\nat daily to weekly intervals by comparing\nits derived\nLWN(_.) with concurrently\nderived in situ LwN(_,) meeting the uncertainty criteria described\nabove.\nThe\nmost direct way of measuring LwN(g) on a continuing daily basis over periods of several\nspecially designed array of radiometers\nmounted on a moored buoy. This buoy must be\nthe optical collectors well away from platform shading and reflections,\nartifacts similar\ndiscussed in Chapter I0 (Sect. 10.2). To minimize uncertainties\ndue to extrapolation\nof\n\nyears is to utilize a\ndesigned to mount\nto ship shadow, as\nupwelling radiance\n\nLu(z,_ ) to the sea surface, the buoy must be moored at a location with consistently transparent\ncase 1 waters\nand with negligible\nmesoscale\nto sub-mesoscale\nspatial variability.\nTo assure frequent occurrences\nof\nmatched satellite and buoy measurements,\nthe site must be cloud free throughout\nmost of the year. The\nmooring\nmust be located close to an island based sun photometer\nand sky radiance\nsensor to allow\nconcurrent determinations\nof aerosol optical thickness and sky radiance distribution.\nOn the other hand, the\natmospheric\nconditions at the mooring location must not be significantly\ninfluenced\nby the island\'s wake.\nExtraordinary\ncalibration\nmaintenance\nprocedures\nare needed to assure low uncertainties\nin the buoy\'s\nradiometric\nmeasurements.\nIn addition, comparative\nshipboard measurements\nmust be made near the buoy\nto check the radiometric\nstability of its instrumentation,\nto determine\nspatial variability\nsurrounding\nthe\nbuoy location, and to develop and validate bio-optical\nalgorithms.\nSome of these measurements\ncan be\nmade during cruises staged to replace the mooring at 3 to 4 month intervals, but dedicated cruises of I to 2\nweek duration\nare also required.\nThe logistical demands of buoy maintenance,\ncalibration\nactivities,\ndeployment\nand relief, and ship support operations strongly argue for placing the buoy conveniently\nnear a\npermanent\nsupport facility.\nThe locations\nof the MOBY mooring, near the island of Lanai, and the\nassociated support facilities in Honolulu, Hawaii closely satisfy all of the above conditions.\n\ncolor\n\nThe radiometric\nsensors differ\n\nmeasurements\nat a primary reference site for vicarious calibration\nof satellite\nin several aspects from the radiometric\nin-water profiling methods described\n\nocean\nin the\n\nChapter 10. A primary reference data set must consist of in situ determinations\nof band-averaged\nLwN(_,)\'s\nthat reproduce\nthe spectral response functions of each satellite sensor\'s bands with more accuracy than can\nbe realized using off the shelf radiometers.\nThe need for flexibility in the choice of spectral response\nweighting\nfunctions\nused to determine\nband-averaged\nLWN(_.) imposes a requirement\nfor full-spectrum\nmeasurements\nwith resolutions\n<1 nm.\nInstead of measuring radiometric\nprofiles resolved at several\nsamples per m (Chapter 10, Sect. I0.2), downwelling\nirradiance Ed(Z, ft) and upwelling radiance Lu(z,$) can\nbe measured\non a buoy at only a few fixed depths, which complicates\nthe problem\nof accurately\ndetermining\n\nLu(0",3.)\n\n(just below the sea surface).\n\n139\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\nTo be affordable,\n\na moored\n\narray\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nmust typically\n\nbe deployed\n\nand operated\n\nsemi-autonomously\n\nfor\n\nperiods\nof 3 to 4 months.\nProvisions\nto assure radiometric\nstability through these extendeM period\noperations\nshould include,\nas a minimum,\npre- and post-deployment\ncalibrations\nof all ra5iometers,\ncombined with continuous monitoring of on-board light sources of known stability.\nMoreover, ir_strurnents\nsuspended in seawater for periods of this duration experience fouling by biological\norganisms that, if not\ncountered\neffectively\nusing antifouling\nmethods and frequent cleaning by divers, seriously degrade the\nperformance\nof optical sensors.\nAffordable servicing and maintenance\nduring each deployment\nis limited\nto circa monthly visits by divers to clean instruments, check sensor calibrations against portable underwater\nlamp sources, and perform minimal maintenance.\nBecause of these uniquely different measurement\nrequirements,\nplatform related geometry, instrument\ncharacteristics,\nand operational\nconditions, the protocols applying to the MOBY instruments\nand methods\nof measurement\nand data analysis are presented\nseparately in the present chapter.\nWhere appropriate,\ncertain protocols will be defined and described by reference to other chapters.\nThis chapter documents\nthe\nprotocols used by the MOBY Operations Team to measure and derive the MOBY data sets that consist of\nthe Lw_(\xc2\xa3) time series. The information\nis intended as background\nfor those wishing to apply this data to\nvalidate water-leaving\nradiances from one or more satellite ocean color sensors.\n\n11.2 THE MOBY\n\nPRIMARY\n\nVICARIOUS\n\nCALIBRATION\n\nSITE\n\nThe primary components\nof the MOBY vicarious calibration site are located as shown in Fig. 11.1. A\nMOBY is continuously\nmoored approximately\n20 km west of the island of Lanai in 1200 m of water.\nDuring prevailing\ntrade wind conditions,\nthis location is sheltered in the lee of the island, yet it is far\nenough offshore to minimize atmospheric\nperturbations\nassociated\nwith the island\'s wake.\nCIMEL 2 sun\nphotometers\non Lanai and Oahu, operated by the Aerosol Robotic Network (AERONET)\nProject (Chapters\n7 and 14), provide time series measurements\nof aerosol optical thickness and sky radiance distributions\nthat\nare required to reduce the uncertainty\nbudgets of atmospheric\ncorrection\nmodels used during vicarious\ncalibration analyses.\nThe MOBY Operation Site, located at the University of Hawaii (UH) Marine Facility\nin Honolulu, is staffed full time by personnel from the Moss Landing Marine Laboratory\n(MLML) for buoy\nmaintenance,\ninstrument maintenance\nand calibration, and for staging buoy relief and bio-optical\nsampling\ncruises. The UH\'s research vessels are used for cruises to support buoy deployments\n(L-series cruises\nidentified\nin Table 11.1), Marine\nOptical Characterization\nExperiments\n(MOCE-series),\nand interim\nmaintenance\nand quality, control operations.\nDuring the MOCE and some L-series cruises (Table 11.1), in\nsitu bio-optical\nmeasurements\nare made to validate MOBY LwN(_.) determinations,\nto characterize\nspatial\nvariability\nnear the mooring, and to develop and validate bio-optical\nalgorithms.\nA subset of the MOBY\ndata is transmitted,\nin real time via cellular telephone, to the MLML in California.\nThe MOBY data are\nprocessed at MLML to produce and extract appropriately\nweighted band-averaged\nLwN(_.)\'s for SIMBIOS\nand SeaWiFS Project Offices at the National Aeronautics\nand Space Administration\n(NASA) Goddard\nSpace Flight Center (GSFC), and/or to the MODIS Team at the University of Miami.\n\nMOBY\n\nand the Marine\n\nMOBY\n\nOptical\n\nSystem (MOS)\n\nis a 12 m spar buoy\n\n(including\n\nthe lower\n\ninstrument\n\nbay) uniquely\n\ndesigned\n\nas an optical\n\nbench for measurements\nof Ed(Z,g) and Lu(z,&) at depths of 1 m, 5 m, 9 m, and 12 mechanical\nlayouts of the upper and lower\ninstrument bays are illustrated\nin Fig. 11.4 and Fig. 11.5, respectively.\nThe MOBY spar is tethered to a\nsecond surface buoy, which is slack moored, i.e. isolated by subsurface floats, to an anchor on the sea floor\n(Fig. 11.6).\nSensors for wind speed, wind direction, air temperature,\nrelative humidity, and barometric\npressure are mounted on the The replacement buoy is then towed into position by the divers\n\n146\n\nOcean\nOptics\n\nFigure\n\n11.7:\n\nProtocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nA schematic\n\ndiagram\n\nof the MOS functional\n\nelements.\n\n(operating from a support boat) and connected\nto the mooring.\nWhen possible, in-water radiometric\nmeasurements are made with both MOBY systems as a final check on the stability of the recovered system.\nFinally, the recovered MOBY is secured aboard the ship for its return to the MOBY Operations\nSite in\nHonolulu.\nThe entire mooring (Fig. 11.6) is replaced at approximately\n1-year intervals (Table 11.4). A new anchor and surface mooring buoy are deployed, and a MOBY is tethered to it.\n\nMOCE\n\nand Other Validation\n\nShipboard\n\nOperations\n\nOn some of the MOBY replacement\ncruises (\'%-cruises"\nin Table 11.l), additional ship time is used to\nmake "Required"\nand "Highly Desired" radiometric\nand bio-optical measurements\n(Chapter 3, Table 3.1),\nboth for radiometric\nvalidation\nof the MOBY and satellite ocean color sensor measurements,\nand for\nalgorithm\n\ndevelopment\n\nand validation.\n\nComplementing\nthe MOBY project are the MOCE\nvicarious calibration\nand validation\nof satellite ocean\nscientists\nfrom the National Oceanic and Atmospheric\nData Information\nService\n(NOAAtNESDIS),\nMLML\nMiami (Florida).\nMOCE cruises, which are typically\nconducted in the general vicinity of the MOBY site and\nMeasurements\n\nacquired\n\nduring\n\nthe MOCE\n\ncruises, which are carried out primarily to support\ncolor sensors.\nThe MOCE team is comprised\nof\nAdministration/National\nEnvironmental\nSatellite\nSan Diego State University,\nand the University\nof\nbetween 10 and 30 days in duration,\nhave been\noff the west coast of North America (Table 11.1).\n\ncruises\n\nare generally\n\nmore\n\nextensive\n\nthan\n\nthose\n\nmade\n\nduring the "L-cruises".\nAs an example, measurements\nmade during MOCE-5 are listed in Table 11.5,\nwhich includes all of the "Required",\nnearly all of the \'q-I_ghly Desired\'_dsbrne\nof the "Specialized\nMeasurements"\ncategories\nof variables\nlisted in Table 3.1 (Chapter\n3).\nProtocols for most of these\nmeasurements\nand analyses conform to those described elsewhere in this document.\nHowever, protocols\n\n147\n=\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nare not provided elsewhere for two of the specialized\nmeasurements\nlisted in Table 11.5, "Instrument\nSelfShading" and "Particle Size Distribution",\nnor are measurements\nof these variables identified in Chapter 3.\nThe effects of instrument\nself-shading\non upwelling\nradiance\nand irradiance\nmeasurements\nare\ndiscussed briefly in Chapter 4 (Sect. 4.2), and provisional\nprotocols (based on Gordon and Ding 1992) for\nremoving\nself-shading\neffects from measurements\nare described\nin Chapter 10 (Sect. 10.4).\nDirect\nmeasurements\nof the self-shading\nphenomenon\nare made during MOCE cruises (Table 11.5) to test the\npredictions\nof Gordon and Ding (1992) and determine\nrelative uncertainties\nunder a variety of ambient\nillumination\nconditions.\nA Fiber Optic Spectrometer\n(FOS) was developed using two modified\nAmerican\nHolographic\nAH4000\nseries dual-beam\nspectrometers,\none configured\nfor radiometric\nmeasurements\nspanning the wavelength\nrange from 375 nm to 725 nm at 5 nm resolution,\nand the other from 600 nm to\n1100 nm at 10 nm resolution.\nThe spectrometers\nare placed in a pressure housing and are coupled by fiberoptic leads to upwelling radiance and downwelling\nirradiance collectors that are located approximately\n1m\naway to minimize shading and reflection effects. The upwelling radiance probe is -5 cm in diameter, and\nthe self-shading effect is varied by attaching discs of increasing diameter to it.\nParticle size distributions\nare measured using a commercial\n(Spectrex) laser particle counter.\nParticle\ncounts, binned by size, are determined\nby measuring the magnitude of reflections\nfrom particles in a small\nworking volume of water illuminated by a Helium-Neon\ndiode (670.8 nm) LASER.\nThe working volume,\nwhich is embedded in a 100 ml water sample, and its geometry are defined by the optical elements of the\ninstrument.\nThe water sample is agitated with a magnetic stirrer to keep particles in suspension during the\nmeasurements.\nThe protocols used for determining\nparticle size distributions\nare those provided by the\nmanufacturer\nof the Spectrex, which claim to resolve particle sizes as small as 1 IJ.m. On the other hand,\nthe assumptions\nunderlying\nthe method are that the individual particles are separated by distances\nlarge\ncompared to the wavelength of illumination,\nand that particle diameters are at least 5 times larger than the\nwavelength,\nso that particle reflection is governed by geometric optics. Other investigators\nhave used the\nSpectrex\ninstrument\nto measure particle size distributions,\nbut a community\nconsensus\nhas yet to be\ndeveloped for protocols related to this measurement\nand its interpretation.\n\nMarine Optical System - Dual Spectrographs\n\nFigure\n\n11.8:\n\nA schematic\n\ndiagram\n\nshowing\n\nthe optical\n\n148\n\ndesign of the MOS spectrographs.\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\n\\\n\nFigure 11.9:\n\nMOBY\nPre-Deployment\n\nFigure\n\n11.10:\n\nMOBY radiometric\n\nDeployment\n\nof a MOBY.\n\nCALIBRATION\n\nDuring Deployment\n\ncalibration\n\nand system\n\n149\n\nPost-Deployment\n\nstability\n\ncheck flow chart.\n\nOcean ProtocolsSatellite\nOptics\nFor\n\nFigure 1 I. 11: The diver-deployed\nradiance collector on MOBY.\nMOBY\n\nSystem\n\nMOBY\ndeployment.\nconnection\nduring each\nradiometric\noverpasses\nRadiometric\n\nOperations\n\nunderwater\n\nradiometric\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nstability\n\nreference\n\nunit attached\n\nto an upwelling\n\nScheduling\n\ndata collection\nis programmed at the MOBY Operations\nSite in Honolulu, prior to each\nAfter the buoy has been deployed, any necessary program changes are made using a direct\nto the buoy\'s on-board computer.\nThe on-board\ncomputer is programmed\nto acquire data\nevent when the mooring site is in view of a satellite ocean color sensor. Currently, the MOBY\nmeasurement\nsequence, described below, is executed twice daily, coincident with the predicted\nof SeaWiFS and MODIS.\nMeasurements\n\nThe MOS\n\nmeasures\n\nradiation\n\nchannel is selected by the\ninput from the multiplexer,\nincandescent\nlamp (Table\ntypically range from 1 s to\n\ninput from one Lu(z,_. ), Ed(z,;t ) or Es(;I. ) head at a time.\n\nThe desired\n\noptical multiplexer.\nA rotating mirror within the MOS selects alternatively\nthe\na dark reference calibration, light emitting diodes (LEDs), or a tungsten halogen\n11.3).\nIntegration\ntimes for the radiance collector\non the top arm, at 1 m,\n4 s for the blue spectrograph and 10 s to 30 s for the red spectrograph.\nA typical\n\nsequence\nwould be to measure Lu(R,z) from a depth, preceded\nand followed by Es(X) surface reference\nspectra and associated dark spectra. Then this sequence is repeated at the 2 "a and 3_a depths to complete the\nprofile\n\nfor Lu(;t,z),\n\nas summarized\n\nin the example\n\nof Table\n\n11.6.\n\nNote\n\nthat\n\nthere\n\nare a total\n\nof 35\n\nmeasurements\nfor radiances\nat the 3 depths, surface irradiance\nEs(_.) and sensor dark spectra.\nThe 35\nmeasurements\nare grouped into overlapping subsets of 15 measurements,\nrepresenting\nthe cycle associated\nwith upwelled radiance measurements\nat each depth. Not included in the example are measurements\nfrom\nthe base of the MOS itself, because these data are not currently used to determine\nwater-leaving\nradiance.\nThis entire procedure requires between 30 min and 1 hr to complete.\n\n150\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nMethods\n\nfor Mitigating\n\nBio-Fouling\n\nMarine organisms,\nincluding algae and barnacles, typically attach themselves\nto any surface that is\nimmersed in seawater for an extended period.\nThis "bio-fouling"\nprocess changes the transmittance\nof the\nradiance windows and irradiance cosine diffusers.\nThe radiance windows are placed at the base of copper\ntubes, and small amounts of bromide are slowly released near the windows throughout\nthe deployment.\nBoth of these substances are poisons for most marine life. It is not practical to use this approach with the\nirradiance\ncollectors, so a combination\nof Teflon collector material, copper bezels and less effective antifouling compounds\nis used here. Divers clean the optical collectors\nmonthly intervals during each deployment.\n\nAncillary\n\nand recharge\n\nthe bromide\n\ndispensers\n\nat\n\nMeasurements\n\nAboard the mooring buoy, the meteorological\nstate variables (wind velocity,\nsurface pressure, air\ntemperature\nand humidity), sea surface temperature and conductivity,\nand near-surface\nin situ chlorophyll\na\nfluorescence,\nare sampled as 5 rain averages at 15 rain intervals, and recorded continuously\non the system\ndisk. The data records are downloaded\nonce daily.\n\nSun Photometer\n\nand Sky Radiance\n\nMeasurements\n\n(on Lanai and Oahu)\n\nThe AERONET Project at NASA GSFC operates the CIMEL 1 sun photometers\non Lanai and Oahu,\nand retrieves the data, remotely. The aerosol optical thickness and sky radiance distribution data measured\nat these sites are needed as input to atmospheric correction\nmodels when the MOBY water leaving\nradiances are used for vicarious calibration of the satellite ocean color sensor. The data are archived by,\nand may be obtained from, the AERONET Project at NASA GSFC. MOBY support personnel visit the\nphotometer sites at monthly intervals to check and clean the instruments.\n\n11.4 CALIBRATION\nThe\n\nMOBY\n\nAND\n\nradiometers\n\nare\n\nQUALITY\n\ncharacterized\n\nCONTROL\n\nand calibrated\n\nusing\n\nprocedures\n\nthat\n\nconform\n\nto the\n\nprotocols described in Chapter 6. The unique role of MOBY as a primary, long term, and daily reference\nfor vicarious calibration\nof satellite ocean color sensors requires radiometric\nmeasurements\nof the highest\npossible\nquality, and\nradiometric\nstandards.\ndirectly\nvalidate\n\nthis in turn places stringent deeds\nFor example, the MOBY team uses\n\non the methods of traceablqity\nexclusively\nirradiance\nstandards\n\nfrom NIST, NIST recalibrates\nthese sources frequendy\nthe team\'s radiometric\nsources at annual intervals.\n\nThe MOS\n\nradiometers\n\nare calibrated\n\nbefore\n\n(see\n\nbelow),\n\nand after each deployment,\n\nand\n\nNIST\n\nand stability\n\nto NIST\nacquired\n\ninvestigators\n\ntests are made\n\nduring deployments\nusing both on-board\nand diver-deployed\nsources.\nThese calibrations,\ntests and\ncomparative\nmeasurements\nare illustrated schematically\nin Figure 11.10. The special aspects of the MOBY\nradiometric\ncalibration, characterization\nand stability test procedures are described in this section.\n\nRadiometric\n\nCalibration\n\nThe spectral\n\nand Characterization\n\nirradiance\n\nresponsivities\n\nof M OS\n\nof the MOS Ed(Z,)_) and Es(_-) channels\n\nare calibrated\n\nusing FEL-\n\ntype lamp standards of spectral irradianee,\nand the Lu(z,$) channels are calibrated using lamp-illuminated\nintegrating\nsphere sources.\nThe wavelength\ncalibration is performed\nusing spectral line emission lamps,\nand every calibration\ncycle includes a measure of three internal sources (see below).\nEach instrument,\nwhether for a MOCE or for a MOBY deployment,\nis calibrated at the support facility site in Honolulu\nbefore and after the in-water deployment.\nThe standards of spectral irradiance and radiance are recalibrated\nevery 50 h of operation.\nThe irradiance standards, I000 W quar[z_:halogen lamps (model nUmber _L),\nare\ncalibrated by NIST. The integrating Sphere source radiance standards are calibrated by their manufacturer,\nOptronic Laboratories,\nInc. The MOS irradiance\nresponsivity\nassignments\nare NIST-traceable\nusing the\nNIST-issued\nFEL lamps.\nDuring the MOS irradiance\ncalibration,\nthe lamps are operated at the correct\ncurrent using a calibrated\nshunt resistor in series with the lamp.\nThe lamp is operated in an enclosed\n\n151\ni\n|\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite Color\nOptics\nFor\nOcean Sensor\nValidation\nhousing the\nat\n\nsame distance and with the same collection area as at NIST (50 cm and 1 cm 2, respectively).\nA reference\nmounting plate ensures the alignment\nof the lamp to the irradiance collector.\nThe validity of\nthis approach was verified by NIST (Mueller et aL 1996).\nThe radiance assignment\nis NIST-traceable\nvia the commercial\nstandards laboratory\'s\ncalibration\nof\nintegrating\nsphere sources (ISSs).\nTwo ISSs are used:\nmodel OL420 and model OL425.\nThey are\nexternally illuminated,\nwith an aperture wheel to vary the radiant output in discrete amounts. However, the\ntwo ISS units differ in the designs of their internal baffles, and in the method used by each to continuously\nvary the output.\nThe ISSs are operated at constant current.\nThe OL425 has, additionally,\nan internal\nilluminance\nmonitor detector that is used to relate the output to that during the calibration measurements\nat\nOptronic Laboratories.\nThe ISSs are re-lamped by Optronic Laboratories,\nand calibrated before and after\nthis procedure,\nso for each sphere and lamp configuration,\nthere is an initial and a final radiometric\ncalibration;\nto date, only the initial calibration values have been used for the MOBY calibrations.\nIn addition to the routine calibration\nof the MOBY radiomelric\nstandards, two single-channel,\ndualmode radiometers\nwere designed and built by NIST to verify the calibrations of the FELs and ISSs and to\nmonitor their stability at the support facility site in Honolulu.\nThese Standard Lamp Monitors (SLMs)\n(Clark et al. 2001) have interchangeable\nforeoptics for operation in either radiance or irradiance mode. The\nnarrowband\n(approximately\n10 nm bandwidth)\ninterference\nfilters are centered at 412 nm and 872 nm.\nDuring every radiometric\ncalibration\nat the support site, the SLMs are used to record the output of the\nradiometric\nstandards.\nThe absolute radiometric response of the SLMs is determined\nby measurements\nat\nNIST, both during the initial development\nand subsequently\non an interval of 12 months to 18 months.\nFinally, site visits by NIST personnel are done at regular intervals, and radiance comparisons\nare\nperformed.\nInitially, the SeaWiFS Transfer Radiometer\n(SXR), a multichannel\nfilter radiometer\n(Johnson\net al. 1998) that is calibrated at NIST, was used. At the present time, a later version of the SXR, the Visible\nTransfer Radiometer\n(VXR) (Johnson et aL 2002) is used, along with a stable, portable, NIST-calibrated\nISS, the NIST Portable Radiance Source (NPR) (Brown and Johnson 2002). The VXR and the NPR were\ndeveloped\nby NIST in support of the calibration program for the Earth Observing System (EOS) (Buffer et\nal. 1999).\n\nMOS Internal\n\nReference\n\nSources\n\nAn internal reference\nsystem was incorporated\ninto the MOS design to monitor the stability of the\nradiometric\ndetectors,\nelectronics,\nand internal optics.\nThese measurements\nare critical for establishing\nconfidence\nin the observations\nacquired during a deployment\ncycle. One of the viewing positions\non the\nmain relay mirror mechanism\nreflects light from a Spectralon I diffuser\nplate into the blue and red\nspectrograph\noptical relays. The diffuser is illuminated in sequence by an incandescent\nlamp, a blue light\nemitting diode (LED), and a red LED.\nThe blue and red diodes are centered at 465 rim and 705 nm,\nrespectively,\nwith approximately\n100 nm bandwidths.\nThe lamps are run with current controlled circuitry\nand the temperature\nof the lamp holder block is monitored.\nThese lamps are observed at the end of each\nMOBY data acquisition\nset (Sect. 11.3). Time histories of reference lamp responses for each deployment\nperiod show the MOS spectrograph\nresponses to be stable at the 1% level.\n\nField\n\nTests of Radiometric\n\nStability\n\nUsing Diver Deployed\n\nSources\n\nThe internal reference lamp and diodes responses (above) do not reflect changes in the throughput\nof\nthe MOS irradiance\nand radiance collectors due to bio-fouling.\nDuring the nominal three-month\nduration\nof a MOBY system deployment\nperiod, a team of divers conducts inspections,\nexternal reference\nlamp\nstability tests, and cleaning monthly.\nDuring these maintenance\noperations, the near-surface components\nof\nthe moored buoy and MOBY are inspected for damage, deterioration,\nand bio-fouling.\nThe condition of the\nbuoy is documented\nwith underwater\nphotography.\nTo document\nthese effects on MOBY radiometry,\nexternal underwater\nreference\nlamp stability baseline measurements\nare conducted\non each irradiance,\nor\nradiance, collector immediately\nafter the MOBY is deployed (Fig. 11.11). The underwater reference lamp\nradiometric\nstability tests are repeated during each monthly service visit, before and after each collector is\ncleaned.\nThe reference lamp system is a modified commercial\nunderwater\nunit using a 35 W incandescent\nlamp.\nThe lamp is powered\nby a submersible,\n12V, 6.5 A h battery-pack.\nModifications\nto the\n\n152\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\ncommercial\nlamp system included\nthe addition of constant current circuitry to the battery pack, and\nconstruction of lamp housings that fit over the radiance and irradiance collectors to block ambient light,\nwhile maintaining a fixed distance between the lamp source and collector.\nThe radiance reference lamp\nsystem has a translucent diffuser placed between the lamp and collector window. Laboratory stability tests\nof the reference lamp systems show the output to be repeatable within 1% if the battery voltage remains\nwithin 25 % of full charge.\nIn the field, batteries are replaced and recharged when voltage decreases to\n50 % of full charge.\nExamples of the diver reference\nlamp responses for a few wavelengths\nat one upwelling radiance\ncollector are illustrated in Fig. 11.12. Data shown at each wavelength represent the difference between the\nMOS responses\non 3 August 2000, when the MOBY was deployed, and the responses before and after\ncleaning on 4 October 2000.\nThe vertical bars illustrate the standard uncertainty in the diver reference\nlamp comparisons.\nBecause all the response measurements\nfall within this uncertainty, the diver lamp\nresponse data have not been used to correct for trends during a deployment.\n\nO\n6.0\n\nI\n\n\'\n\n4.0\n\n|\n\n\'\n\ni\n\n\'"\n\n,\n\nI\n\n\'-\n\n\'\n\nT\nI\n!\n\n!\n\n2.0\n0.0\n\n-2.0\n\n-4.0\n\n_6._\n\n,\n\nI\n\n\'\n\nI\n\n500\n\n,\n\nI\n\n600\nWavelength\n\n,\n\nI\n\n700\n\n\'\n\n800\n\n(rim)\n\n900\n\nFigure relative\n\nspectral\n\nmeasurements\n\narises\n\nISS (radiance\n\nshape of Lu(z,A),\n\nbecause\n\nmode),\n\nof the large\n\nor the FEL lamp\n\nor Ed(Z,A), Sensor\nValidation\ndonothave\n\nan ideal spectral selectivity,\ni.e., the response\nat a wavelength\nof interest to flux at other\nwavelengths\nis small but finite (Chapter 5, Sect. 5.2 and Chapter 6, Sect. 6.4). As a result, measurements\nat\nthe wavelength\nof interest include both a component that is proportional\nto the flux at that wavelength\n(e.g.,\nthe "in-band" component)\nplus a component that sums the product of the sensor response and the, spectrum\nof flux at wavelengths\noutside the in-band region. The latter sum, representing the out-of-band\ncomponent,\nmust be evaluated for all wavelengths\nfor which the detector has finite responsivity.\nFor MOS, the out-ofband response is largely determined by the scattering properties\nof the grating and unwanted reflections\nof\nflux diffracted in second order. We refer to the effect as "stray light".\nStray light considerations\nfor MOS motivated dividing the spectrum\nbeamsplitter\nand two spectrographs.\nAs seen by the blue spectrograph,\nmatch between\nLu(z,_),\n\nthe spectral\n\nand minimizes\n\n440 nm, for example,\nusing independent\n\nshapes of the FEL-type\n\nstray light effects\ncomparisons\n\nfilter radiometers\n\nspectral\n\nin the critical\n\nof Lu(z_X) for MOCE\n\nirradiance\nocean\n\nsources\n\ncolor\n\nor MOBY\n\ninto two regions using a dichroic\nthis division results in a better\nand Ed(Z,2),\n\nwavelength\n\ndeployments\n\nbands.\n\nor the ISSs and\nAt 412 nm and\n\nagree with measurements\n\nto within +_5%.\n\nThe effect of stray light in MOS is most evident in the region of overlap between\nthe two\nspectrographs,\nfrom 545 rim to 650 run.\nFor the red spectrograph,\nthe decreased transmittance\nof the\ndichroic bearnsplitter\nin this region, where it goes from zero to nearly unity transmittance,\nmeans that the\nratio of the in-band to the out-of-band\ncomponents\nis unfavorable.\nIndeed, for some MOS wavelengths\n(CCD columns) at the blue side of the red spectrograph,\nthe recorded signals can be dominated by the outof-band\ncomponent.\nAt the present time, the processing\nalgorithms\nuse the results from the blue\nspectrograph\nup to 620 nm, and those from the red spectrograph\nbeyond 620 nm. The differences\nare\ngenerally stable in time and depend on depth, another indication that the effect is related to source spectral\nshape.\nTo correct for stray light, the function that describes the sensitivity to flux at wavelengths\nother than\nthe desired wavelength\nmust be determined.\nThis requires a tunable, monochromatic\nsource that fills the\nentrance pupil of the sensor.\nImprovements\nin technology\nand the recent addition of new facilities at\nNIST have made it possible to fully characterize\nsensors such as CCD spectrographs\nusing fully tunable\nlaser-illuminated,\nintegrating sphere sources (Brown, Eppeldauer,\nand Lykke 2000). The facility is called\nSpectral Lrradiance and Radiance responsivity\nCalibrations with Uniform Sources (SIRCUS).\nA thorough\nstray light characterization\nstudy for the MOBY project was begun in early 2000. Work\nstarted on SIRCUS\nwith a MOS bench unit (Habauzit\net al. 2002), and continued\nwith the full\ncharacterization\nof MOS202 (which is used as a profiler instrument).\nMeasurements\nare ongoing for the\nMOBY MOS units.\nThe SIRCUS measurements\nyield the absolute radiance response of the sensors.\nExamples\nof the response of a single column in the blue spectrograph\n(column 380), and of the red\nspectrograph\n(column 277), are shown in Fig. 11.13 and Fig. 11.14 for the MOS profiler.\nThe smalI peak\nnear 510 nm for the blue spectrograph\nis caused by flux diffracted in second order and MODIS are transmitted\nto the SeaWiFS and\nOffices, where they are archived\nin SeaBASS\n(Chapter\n18).\nThese data are also\narchived by the MODIS Team at the University\nof Miami. All data recorded by the\non MOCE and other cruises are archived at MLML in Moss Landing, California and at\nCamp Springs, Maryland.\n\n11.7 FUTURE DIRECTIONS\nTemperature\n\nCharacterizations\n\nAlthough the MOS CCDs are temperature-controlled,\nthe temperatures\nof the optical components\nin\nthe spectrographs,\nthe electronics,\nthe MOBY fiber optics, and other system components\nare subject to\nenvironmental\nconditions.\nThese ancillary instrument temperatures\nare recorded and archived, but are not\nused in the present data processing\nalgorithms.\nBecause the ambient temperature\nand degree of thermal\nequilibrium\ndepends\non the measurement\npurpose (calibration\nvs. in-water\nradiometry)\nand type of\ndeployment\n(MOBY vs. MOS), the radiometric\nresponsivities\nof MOBY and MOS are being studied as a\n\n160\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite Ocean\nOptics\nFor\n\nfunction\nMOS.\n\nof temperature.\n\nStray Light\n\nVarious\n\ntemperature-controlled\n\nColor Sensor Validation\n\nbaths\n\nare used, including\n\none large\n\nenough\n\nfor\n\nCharacterizations\n\nThe MOBY Project includes a suite of instruments,\nrequiring multiple field deployments to address the\nstray light issues.\nOnce the required data are in hand, the stray light algorithm for all of the required\ninstruments\nwill be optimized.\nThis may require separate model parameters\nfor each input collector on\nMOBY.\nThen, the MOCE and MOBY data sets will be reprocessed,\nleading to an improvement\nin the\naccuracy of the derived LwN(_,)\'s that are used for vicarious calibration of MODIS, SeaWiFS, and other\nocean color satellite sensors.\nIt is anticipated that the uncertainty\nin the correction will be about 10 % of\nthe effect, e.g. the uncertainty\n\nin the corrected\n\nvalues from stray light would be 0.5 % for a 5 % correction.\n\nACKNOWLEDGMENTS\nThe NIST efforts are Comparisons have also been made between each\nmethod and RRs(_,) determined from in-water Lu(z,_,) and above-water Es(_,;0o) measurements\n(e.g. Rhea\nand Davis, 1997; Mueller et al. 1997; Fougnie et aL 1999; Hooker et al. 1999), finding root-mean-square\ndifferences generally larger than 20 % for any sample spanning a reasonably large range of environmental\nconditions.\nSome of these investigators have normalized the measurements,\nusing the method of Morel and\nGentili (1996) to account for variations in viewing and solar zenith angles and in the ocean Bidirectional\nReflectance\nDistribution Function (BRDF), prior to making the comparisons (e.g. Mueller et aI. 1997;\nToole et al. 2000), and some have not (e.g. Rhea and Davis 1997; Fougnie et al. 1999).\n\nMethod\n\n1: Calibrated\n\nRadiometers\nare used\nchosen\n\nradiance\n\nwhich\n\nto measure\nto estimate\n\nand irradiance\n\nmeasurements.\n\nhave been fully characterized\n\nLac (X,O,\xc2\xa2 _ _mv ;0o),\nsurface\n\nreflectance\n\nand calibrated,\n\nL_ (_l,,e_,\xc2\xa2_\n\np, and\n\nfollowing\n\nthe methods\n\ne f_ov ;oo ) and Es(_.;0o).\n\n/_w(_,,0,\xc2\xa2ef_rov;0o)\n\nand\n\nof Chapter\n\nAssumptions\n\n6,\nare\n\nRRs(3.,O,\xc2\xa2_f2rov;O,)are\n\ncalculated\nusing equations\n(12.1) and (12.2).\nExample\nimplementations\nof this straightforward\ninstrumental\napproach,\nand comparisons\nwith remote sensing reflectance\ndetermined\nfrom in-water\nmeasurements,\nare described in Rhea and Davis (1997), Mueller et al. Especially\nwhile steaming, ocean color radiance measurements\nshould usually be made from the bow, because from this location it is practical to view a spot where the\nwater is undisturbed\nby the ship\'s wake or associated foam. It must also be easy, in the selected position, to\npoint in a direction away from the sun to reduce specular reflection of sunlight.\nTo measure\nangles,\n\n_tc (;L,0,\xc2\xa2\n\nmeasured\n\nat the pixel,\n\nin all circumstances\nFor polarized\nviewing\nmay\n\nazimuth\n\na viewing\n\nby the g/ory\n\nconfiguration.\nMeasurements\nreasons discussed in Section\ncoverage\n\nshould be pointed\n\n(0, _) = (40-45 \xc2\xb0, 135\xc2\xb0), if possible\n\nthe viewing\n\nmeasurements\n\n) the radiometer\n\nbe avoided\n\nduring\n\nmay unavoidably\n\ntoward the sea surface at viewing\n\n(Mobley\n\n1999; Fougnie\n\nmust be in the range 90 \xc2\xb0 < _ < 180 \xc2\xb0 relative\nangle of 0 = 45 \xc2\xb0 is strongly\n\nangle that is 180 \xc2\xb0 away from the sun\'s azimuth\n\nbe contaminated\n\nshould\n\n_ f_ov;0o\n\nphenomenon,\n\nrecommended\n\nshould be avoided.\n\nand\n\nship\n\nshadow\n\net aI. 1999), and\n\nto the sun\'s azimuth.\n\n(Fougnie\n\nThe measurements\n\nmight\n\nalso\n\net al. 1999). In addition, whitecaps, foam and floating material\nmeasurements,\ncontaminate\n\nbut at wind\n\nspeeds\n\nexceeding\n\n10 m s -1 extensive\n\nwhitecap\n\nthe data record to some extent.\n\nBecause of temporal variability\nin surface reflectance, due to wind gusts and waves, it is important to\nrecord a number of spectra within a period of several seconds, or minutes if necessary. With filter\nradiometers\n(Mueller\n1997; Fougnie et al. 1999; Hooker et al. 1999, 2000), it is feasible to sample\nindividual\nspectra at rates of several Hz, and the electronic\ngain changes\naccount for the different\nmagnitudes\nof the water and sky signals.\n\n174\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite Ocean\nOptics\nFor\n\nColor Sensor Validation\n\nIf miniature, fiber-optic spectroradiometers\nare used, on the other hand, the debtor\nintegration time is\nvaried to provide the necessary dynamic range. Sky radiances may be integrated over a few hundred msec,\nwhile the ocean surface radiance may be integrated over 1 to 2 see. A separate dark reading must be\nobtained each time the integration\ntime is changed.\nA typical measurement\nsequence with this type of\nspectroradiometer\nis to measure plaque-reflected,\nsea and sky and radiances (each preceded by a dark offset\nreading), in that order, and repeat the sequence 5 or more times.\nData records of longer duration may be advisable to improve averaging over modulation of capillary\nwaves by wind variability\nand gravity waves, but there has been little research on that aspect of the\nproblem.\nBefore calculating\nfinal mean and standard deviation\nspectra, positive outliers due to briefly\nviewed foam patches, whitecaps and strong glint should be removed by inspection of the data record.\nWhen using Method\na sun photometer\n\n3, described\n\nmeasurement,\n\nand\n\nabove, only\n\n/__ (_,0,0\n\nLa-r (3.,0_,_\n\n\xc2\xa2 _mv;0.)need\n\ne f_ov;0,)and\n\nbe measured,\n\nEs(7_;0o) are modeled.\n\nbe done accurately\nwhen clouds do not obscure the solar disk and fractional\nThese are the necessary conditions for the measurement_\nTo measure\n\nL_ (_,0_,_\n\n\xc2\xa2 \xc2\xa3_ov ;0o )(Methods\n\ntogether\n\nwith\n\nThis can only\n\ncloud cover is less than 20 %.\n\n1 and 2), the radiometer\n\nis pointed\n\nupward\n\nto view\n\nthe sky at angles (0sky, _s_) = (0, _), e.g. (40 \xc2\xb0, 135\xc2\xb0). The radiometer\nshould be mounted in a location that is free of both shadows and reflections\nof\nlight from any part of the ship\'s superstructure\n(see also Section 10.2). This can usually be accomplished\nby mounting the radiometer\nhigh on a mast, albeit in some combinations\nof location and ship\'s heading,\nintermittent\nshadowing\nby antennas,\nstays and other parts of the ship\'s rigging may contaminate\nthe\nEs(L;0o) measurements.\nThe data must also be edited to remove\nmeasurements\nwhen the irradiance\ncollector\'s\norientation\nis more than 50 away from horizontal. In this approach,\nan accurately\ncharacterized\nBRDF for the plaque is as critical as are the accuracies\nof radiometric\ncalibrations\nin Methods 1 and 3. Traditionally,\ngray reflectance plaques with approximately\n10 % nominal\nreflectance have been used for this measurement\n(Carder and Steward 1985; Rhea and Davis 1997; Hooker\net al. 1999), but white Spectralon plaques with 99 % reflectance offer better homogeneity\nin BRDF (over\nthe plaques surface area) and have been used by some investigators\n(e.g. Hooker et al. 1999; Toole et al.\n2000).\nThe plaque must be held horizontally,\nand exposed to the sun and sky in a position free from both\nshading by, and reflections from, any part of the ship\'s superstructure,\nobserver, or radiometer.\nIt may be\ndifficult, on some ships, to find a location that meets this requirement\nand also affords an unobstructed\nview of the sea surface at an acceptable\n(0, #p) relative to the sun. In such situations,\nthe alternative\napproaches\nshould be considered.\nWith the horizontal plaque thus located, it is viewed by the radiance\nsensor at the angles consistent\nwith the solar direction\nand the plaque\'s\nBRDF characterization.\nThe\nsimplest approach is to determine the BRDF for the sensor view normal to the plaque center and use that\nviewing geometry in the field. Finally, the radiance reflected from the plaque is recorded.\n\nSun Photometer\n\nMeasurements\n\nIt is strongly recommended\nthat sun photometer measurements\nbe made to determine\naerosol The Mueller and Austin (1995) protocols\nrelated to above-water measurements\nof water-leaving radiance and remote-sensing\nreflectance should not\nbe followed\n\nunder any circumstances.\n\nThe above-water\nmethods for determining\nnormalized\nremote-sensing\nreflectance\n(NRSR) 3, as\ndescribed\nabove, and their associated\nuncertainty budgets, have been discussed\nat length in several\nmeetings and workshops over the last few years, as well as in the literature cited here. In particular, a\nSIMBIOS sponsored NRSR Workshop was held at Old Dominion University (Norfolk, VA) in December\n1997. At that workshop, the participants agreed that the uncertainty budgets associated with the abovewater methods proposed for determining NRSR are poorly known, and that a unified data set was needed as\na basis for correcting that deficiency.\nIt was also the workshop consensus\nthat additional research and\nanalyses should be pursued to:\n1.\n\nDetermine uncertainties in and between Es(L;0o) determined by a) direct measurement\nwith a\ncalibrated radiometer (Method i), b)estimation\nbased on measurement\nof radiance reflected\nfrom a gray target have a known BRDF (Method 2), and radiative transfer models for clear\nsky conditions (Method 3), with and without independent measurements\nof aerosol and ozone\noptical Conversely,\nin a muidple scattering regime, the highly\ndiffuse light field tends to become isotropic.\nThe more or less diffuse character of the upward radiant field\nactually depends\non the average number,\nfi, of scattering events the photons have undergone\nbefore\nreaching the interface (z = 0). This number is simply related to the IOP through (Morel and Gentili, 1991)\n_(X) = [1-_(_)]-\',\n\nwhere\n\n_(_\')--\n\nalternatively\n\nTherefore,\n\nb(_,)\na (_)+b(_,)\nbe written\n\nis the sing/e scatter/ag\n\n_(_)\n\nin a strongly\n\nb(k)\n=1+ a--_\'\nscattering\n\nalbedo.\n\n(13.15)\nIt is straightforward\n\nto show that (13A5)\n\nmay\n\nor_(j_)=a[L)+b(_,)"\na(_,)\nmedium\n\n_ is close to 1, h is extremely\n\nhigh, multiple\n\nscattering\n\nof\n\nhigh orders is the common rule, and finally the radiance distribution\ntends to be isotropic.\nConversely,\nin a\nstrongly absorbing\nmedium, _ is small, h is not much above 1, single scattering dominates\nover higher\norders, and the radiance pattern tends to mimic that of the VSF (single scattering).\nIn such a case, if\nmolecular\nscattering dominates\n(rl large, and rib tending toward 1), the shape of the VSF is more or less\nsymmetrical\nand the radiance field is accordingly\nrather round-shaped,\nbut if particle scattering dominates\n(r I small, and rib tending toward 0), the shape of the VSF is extremely elongated in the forward direction\nand the radiance field is more structured.\nFigure 13.3 illustrates _ hypo-tfie_ai\nexamples of the upward\nradiant field and its bidirectional\ncharacter, for optically pure water (no particles),\nor for marine particles\nalone (without water); the average number of scattering events is either 2, or 61, and the sun is at zenith, or\n60 \xc2\xb0 away from zenith. The conventions\nin the polar plots of Figure 13.3 are the same as in Figure 13.2.\n\nFluorescence\n\nand Raman\n\nScattering\n\nIn all that has been said before, the only physical processes considered were absorption\nand scattering.\nIn other words the medium was considered as purely passive. Other phenomena\noccur, when the medium\ncontains sources, which in seawater are mainly fluorescence\nemission and Raman scattering.\nFluorescence\nemission creates light at certain wavelengths,\ntypical of the substance responsible\nfor the\nemission.\nFor instance, Chlorophyll\na (borne by algal cells) fluoresces around 683 nm with a bandwidth\nof\nabout 25 nm, whereas the fluorescence\nemitted by colored dissolved organic matter (CDOM)\nis more\ncomplex and spectrally broader.\nFluorescence\nmay be excited by photons at any wavelength\nshorter than\nthat of the emission,\nalthough the excitation efficiency\nmay vary with wavelength\n(due largely to the\nabsorption\nspectrum\nof the material in question).\nThese emissions\nare obviously\ndependent\non the\nconcentration\nof the fluorescing\nsubstances.\nThe Raman (inelastic) scattering is characterized\nby a constant frequency shift between the excitation\nand emitted light, and the shift is only determined\nby the molecule structure of the medium (here water).\nThis emission occurs throughout the visible spectrum, at wavelengths\nlarger than those of the exciting light.\nBecause it is a physical property of the water molecule, it is always present, even if its influence is hardly\ndetected in the upper layers, where the elastic scattering process dominates the upward radiant field.\nFrom the viewpoint\nof the directional\nproperties\nof the upward field, these emissions,\nisotropic\nfor\nfluorescence\nand quasi-isotropic\nfor Raman scattering, add a component that is angularly constant and thus\ntends to smooth out the structures generated by elastic scattering (particularly\nby particle scattering).\nNote\nthat the coefficients\ndescribing these emissions (the reverse of absorption) belong to the category of iOP\n(see also Chapter 10, Section 10.4).\n\n189\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\nForSatellite Color\nOcean Sensor\nValidation\n\n_=2 (Petzold)\n\nOo= These transformations\nrequire\nthe quantities\n\nL,\nQ\n\nQ\n\n(or\n\nf ), and 91.\n\nConveniently,\n\nthese values\n\ncan be determined\n\nby interpolation\n\nwithin precomputed\nlookup tables, which are built by solving the RTE under specified\nboundary\nconditions,\nusing the appropriate\nIOP describing the water bodies of interest. For the last point, various\nparameterizations\nare possible, so that everybody\nmay have his own preference\nand can produce his own\nlookup tables. Very likely several of these will be built in the near future.\nThe lookup tables succinctly described below are ready for use, and may be obtained over the Internet\n(see below).\nThese tables offer investigators\nan option for parameterizing\nthe !OP of Case 1 waters as\nfunctions of the chlorophyll\nconcentration,\nas described in Morel and Maritorena\n(2001), and summarized\nbelow. The first type is\na photometric\nmeasurement\nof the direct solar beam to determine\nthe optical thickness of the atmosphere.\nThe intensity of the solar beam can be measured directly, or obtained indirectly from measurements\nof\ndiffuse global upper hemispheric\nirradiance.\nThe second type is a measurement\nof the solar aureole and\nsky radiance distribution\nusing a CCD camera, or a scanning radiometer viewing in and perpendicular\ntO\nthe solar principal plane.\nFrom the two types of measurements,\nthe optical properties\nof aerosols, highly variable in space and\ntime, can be derived. Because of the high variability,\nthe aerosol properties should be known at the time of\nsatellite overpass. Atmospheric\noptics measurements,\nhowever, are not easy to perform at sea, from a ship\nor any platform.\nThis complicates\nthe measurement\nprotocols\nand data analysis_ Some instrumen_ti0fi\ncannot be deployed\nat sea, and is limited to island and coastal sites, in the following,\nmeasurement\nprotocols are described\nfor radiometers\ncommonly used to measure direct atmospheric\ntransmittance\nand\nsky radiance,\nnamely standard sun phot0meters _ fast-rotating\nshadow-band\nradiometers,\nautomated\nsky\nscanning systems, and CCD cameras. Also discussed are methods of data analysis and quality control, as\nwell as proper measurement\nstrategies for evaluating atmospheric\ncorrection algorithms and atmospheric\nparameters derived from satellite ocean color measurements.\n\n14.1 AUTOMATIC\nSUN PHOTO_TER\nSCANNING\nSYSTEMS\n\nAND SKY RADIANCE\n\nThe technology of ground-based\natmospheric aerosol measurements\nusing sun photometry has changed\nsubstantially since Volz (1959) introduced the fwst hand-held analog instrument almost four decades ago.\nModern digital units of laboratory quality and field hardiness collect data more accurately and quickly and\nare often equipped for onboard processing (Schmid et al. 1997; Ehsani 1998, Forgan 1994; and Morys et al.\n1998). The method used remains the same, i.e., a detector measures through a spectral filter the extinction\nof direct beam solar radiation according to the Bccr-Lambert-Bouguer\nlaw:\n\nV(,_) = Vo(;L)(-_12 exp [-(\'\xc2\xa2(_,)M)]tg\n(t),\n\n211\n\n(14.1)\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nwhere V(2,) is the measured digital voltage, Vo(2") is the extra-terrestrial\n"t(2\') is the total optical depth, 2, is wavelength, d and do are respectively\ndistances, and ts(2,) is the transmission\nRayleigh and aerosol optical depth.\nThe earth-sun\n\ndistance\n\ncorrection\n\nof absorbing\n\nis calculated\n\n(__)2=\nwhere J is the number\n\nozone, and aerosol\n\ngases. The total optical\n\nusing the approximation\n\nof the sun zenith\n\nangle.\n\nCurrently,\n\ncos _\n180o +0.15"\n\nwhere the sun zenith angle 0o is expressed\n\n(14.2)\n\n1983).\n\nfactors. Air mass is calculated\n\nM =\n\ndepth is the sum of thc\n\n1+ 0.034 cos 2_ .j \'\n365\n\nof the day of the year (Iqbal\n\nAir mass M is a function\nRayleigh,\n\nvoltage, M is the optical air mass,\nthe actual and average ea\',lh-sun\n\nthe same value\n\nof air mass is used for\n\nas\n\n(93.885\n\n- 0o)\\-Lz53]-I ,\n_\n\n(14.3)\n\nin degrees.\n\nSky-scanning\nspectral radiometers\nthat measure the spectral sky radiance at known angular distances\nfrom the sun have expanded\nthe aerosol knowledge\nbase. They provide, through inversion\nof the sky\nradiance, aerosol physical properties,\nsuch as size distribution,\nand optical properties,\nsuch as the aerosol\nscattering phase function (Nakajima et al., 1983, 1996; Tanrd et al., 1988; Shiobara et al., 1991; Kaufman\net al., 1994; Dubovik et al., 2000; and Dubovik and King, 2000). The inversion technique to calculate these\naerosol properties\nrequires precise aureole measurements\nnear the solar disk and good stray-light\nrejection.\nHistorically\nthese systems are cumbersome,\nexpensive,\nand not weather hardy.\nThe CIMEL and PREDE\n(French and Japanese manufacturers\nrespectively)\nsun and sky scanning spectral radiometers\novercome\nmost of such limitations,\nproviding\nretrievals\nof aerosol and water vapor abundance\nfrom direct sun\nmeasurements,\nand of aerosol properties from spectral sky radiance measurements.\nSince the measurements\nare directional\nand represent conditions\nof the total column atmosphere,\nthey are directly applicable\nto\nsatellite and airborne observations,\nas well as to studies of atmospheric processes. When equipped with a\nsophisticated\ntracking system with fast responding\nmotors, the PREDE can be instaUed onboard a ship, or\nother moving platform,\nto monitor aerosol optical properties\nat sea. In the following,\nwe focus on the\nCIMEL system, since the measurement\nprotocols are similar for both CIMEL and PREDE systems.\n\nDescription\nThe CIMEL Electronique\n318A spectral radiometer,\nmanufactured\nin Paris, France, is a solar powered,\nweather hardy, robotically pointed sun and sky spectral radiometer.\nAt each wavelength,\nthis instrument has\napproximately\na 1.2 \xc2\xb0 field-of-view\n(full angle) and filtered solar aureole and sky radiance. The 33 cm\ncollimators\nwere designed for 10 "s stray-light\nrejection for measurements\nof the aureole 3 \xc2\xb0 from the sun.\nThe robot mounted sensor head is pointed at nadir when idle to prevent contamination\nof the optical\nwindows from rain and foreign particles. The sun/aureole\ncollimator\nis protected\nby a quartz window,\nallowing observation\nwith an ultraviolet\nenhanced\nsilicon detector\nwith sufficient\nsignal-to-noise\nfor\nspectral observations\nbetween 300 and 1020 rim. The sky collimator has the same 1.2 \xc2\xb0 field of view, but\nuses an order of magnitude\nlarger aperture-lens\nsystem to improve dynamic range for measuring the sky\nradiance. The components\nof the sensor head are sealed from moisture and packed in dessicant to prevent\ndamage to the electrical components\nand interference\nfilters. Eight ion assisted deposition\ninterference\nfilters are located in a filter wheel rotated by a direct drive stepping motor. A thermistor\nmeasures the\ntemperature\nof the detector, allowing compensation\nfor any temperature dependence in the silicon detector.\nA polarization\nmodel of the CE-318 is also used in SIMBIOS.\nThis version executes\nthe same\nmeasurement\nprotocol as the standard model, but makes additional hourly measurements\nof polarized sky\nradiance at 870 nm in the solar principal plane (Table 14.1 and 14.2).\n\n212\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nInstallation\nThe installation procedures for the CIMEL instrument are summarized below. More\ninformation is available from the AERONET web page (http:flaeronet.gsfc.nasa.gov:8080).\n\ndetailed\n\nThe site should have a clear horizon and be representative of the regional aerosol regime. The basic\nassembly is relatively simple to mount. The cables are labeled clearly and most fit only in one place. Once\nthe robot is assembled, it should be oriented so the zenith motor casing is pointing roughly east (the metal\nclaw to which the sensor head is attached, then points to the west). The round connector end of the data\ncable should be attached to the sensor head, and the flat connector should be plugged into the white CIMEL\ncontrol box. Strap the sensor head to the robot metal claw using the silver metal band. Make sure that the\nface of the sensor head is flush with the edge of the metal claw. Also, ensure that the long axis of the\ncollimator cross-section is perpendicular to the axis of the zenith motor casing and claw. Verify that the\nrobot itself is level. Do not use the embedded bubble level on top of the robot. Place the supplied bubble\nlevel on top of the fiat ledge of the central robot tubular body (below the sensor head motor) This should be\nlevel in both the N/S and E/W axes. Verify that the CIMEL control box "TIME" and "DATE" are correct,\ni.e., that they agree with the VITEL transmitter clock. If the Time or Date is wrong, the CIMEL will no_..!t\nfind the sun on a "GOSUN" command.\nNext, put the CIMEL in manual mode using the white control box display screen. In Manual mode, the\nmain screen reads: "PW MAN SCN VIEW". Do a "PARK" procedure. When "PARK" is complete the\nsensor head collimator should be pointing down, perpendicular to the ground. Place the bubble level on the\ntop of the metal claw arm and verify that this is level. If not, loosen the zenith bolt\'s hex nut (below the\npermanent bubble level on the top of the robot) and level it by rotating the zenith motor casing with your\nhand. Re-tighten the zenith nut tightly. It is important to perform another "PARK" procedure, or two, and\nmake sure it is in fact level.\nUsing the right 2 buttons, change the display to read "GOSUN". Select "GO" to initiate. The sensor\nhead should point to the sun. The hole at the top of the collimator should allow the sunlight to illuminate\nthe marker spot at the base of the collimator. When the bright spot is on the mark, the instrument is aligned.\nIf it is off to the left or right, rotate the robot base to align it. After you rotate the robot, you will need to\nverify that the robot is still level as before. Park the instrument and perform another "GOSUN" to check\nthat the alignment is still good. If not, ensure that the robot is level, and that the sensor head is level when\nmanually parked. One note: when you level the sensor head and do a "GOSUN", repeat this process a few\ntimes to be sure of the alignment. The first "GOSUN" after leveling is often not correct, because moving\nthe sensor head while leveling can temporarily offset the robot\'s zeroing point. Re-parking the sensor and\ndoing a second "GOSUN" should yield a more accurate alignment. Repeat this procedure until the\nalignment remains accurate and consistent on repetition.\nPress "PW" then increment to 4, and place the instrument in "AUTO" mode. The main "AUTO" mode\ndisplay should read: \'\'PW AUTORUN VIEW". The CIMEL should be left in this mode in order to perform\nautomatic measurement sequences.\nThe V1TEL transmitter has a multi-level menu with \'_IME DATE" etc intop level, and sub categories\nbelow each top-level item. The exact menu structure varies with software version (2.01, 2.9, and 2.i i),\nRefer to the version most similar to your particular transmitter. One may operate the _L\ndisplay by\nusing the control buttons. To initiate an action, press the "SET-UP" button, then press the "SCROLL"\nbutton repeatedly to view the categories in the current menu level. To choose any subcategory, press the\n"SELECT\' button when the desired feature is shown in the display window. To change a parameter use\nthe right 2 buttons "CHANGE" and "ENTER\'". At any time, one may return to the previous (higher) menu\nlevel by pressing the "SET-UP" button.\nMeasurement Protocols\nThe radiometer makes only two basic measurements, either direct solar flux, or sky radiance.\ntype of measurement involves several programmed sequences.\n\nEach\n\nDirect sun measurements are made in eight spectral bands distributed between 340 and 1020 rim (440,\n670, 870, 940 and 1020 nm are standard). Each measurement requires approximately I0 seconds. A\nsequence of three such measurements are taken 30 seconds apart creating a triplet observation per\n\n213\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nwavelength.\nTriplet observations\nare made during morning and afternoon Langley calibration\nsequences\nand at standard 15-minute intervals in between (Table 14.1). The time variation of clouds is typically much\ngreater than that of aerosols, and therefore significant variation in the triplets may be used to screen cloudcontaminated\nmeasurements\nfrom the data. Variability\nover the 15-minute interval also allows another\ncheck for cloud contamination\nat a lower frequency.\nSky measurements\nare performed\nat 440, 670, 870 and 1020 nm (Table 14.1).\nA single spectral\nmeasurement\nsequence\n(Langley\nsky) is made immediately\nafter the Langley\nair mass direct sun\nmeasurement,\nwith the sensor pointed 20 \xc2\xb0 from the sun. This is used to assess the stability of the Langley\nplot analysis (O\'Neill\net al. 1984).\nTwo basic sky observation\nsequences are made, "almucantar"\nand\n"principal plane".\nThe objective of these sequences is to retrieve size distribution,\nphase function and\naerosol optical thickness (AOT). This is approached\nby acquiring aureole and sky radiance observations\nspanning a large range of scattering angles, relative to the sun\'s direction,\nassuming a constant aerosol\nprofile.\nAn "almucantar\nsequence" is a series of measurements\ntaken at the same sun elevation for specified\nazimuth angles relative to the Sun position. The range of scattering angles decrease as the solar zenith angle\ndecreases, thus almucantar sequences made at an optical air mass of 2, or more, achieve scattering angles of\n120 \xc2\xb0, or larger. Scattering angles of 120 \xc2\xb0 are typical of many sun-synchronous\nviewing satellites, and thus a\nmeasure of the satellite path radiance is approximated\nfrom the ground station. During an almucantar\nmeasurement,\nobservations\nfrom a single channel are made in a sweep at a constant elevation angle across\nthe solar disk and continue through 360 \xc2\xb0 of azimuth in about 40 seconds (Table 14.2). This is repeated for\neach channel to complete an almucantar\nsequence.\nA direct sun observation\nis also made during each\nspectral almucantar\nMore\n\nthan\n\nsequence.\n\nfour almucantar\n\nsequences\n\nare made daily\n\nat optical\n\nair masses\n\nof 4, 3, 2 and 1.7, both\n\nmorning and afternoon.\nAn almucantar sequence is also made hourly between 9 AM and 3 PM local solar\ntime for the standard instrument and skipping only the noon almucantar for the polarization\ninstrument.\nThe standard\nprincipal\nplane sky radiance\nmeasurement\nsequence\nis similar to the almucantar\nsequence, but the sensor scans in the principal plane of the sun, and therefore all angular distances from the\nsun are scattering angles, regardless of solar zenith angle. This measurement\npointing sequence begins with\na sun observation,\nmoves 6\xc2\xb0 below the solar disk then sweeps through the sun\'s principal plane, taking\nabout 30 seconds for each of the four spectral bands (Table 14.2). Principal plane observations\nare made\nhourly when the optical air mass is less than 2 to minimize the variations in radiance due to the change in\noptical air mass.\nPolarization\nmeasurements\nof the sky at 870 nm are an option with this instrument. The sequence is\nmade in the principal plane at 5 \xc2\xb0 increments between zenith angles of -85 \xc2\xb0 and +85 \xc2\xb0. The configuration\nof\nthe filter wheel requires that a near-IR polarization\nsheet be attached to the filter wheel. Three spectrally\nmatched 870 nm filters are positioned in the filter wheel exactly 120 \xc2\xb0 apart. Each angular observation\nis a\nmeasurement\nof the three polarization filter positions.\nAn observation takes approximately\n5 seconds and\nthe entire sequence about 3 minutes. This sequence occurs immediately\nafter the standard measurement\nsequence in the principal plane.\n\nData Analysis\nThe present protocols\nadopt the data analysis procedures\n(Holben et al, 1998), with specific components\nand characteristics\nThe\nnetwork,\n\nestablished\nfor the AERONET\nsummarized in Table 14.3.\n\nAERONET\nalgorithms\nimpose a processing\nstandardization\non all of the data\nthus facilitating comparison of spatial and temporal data between instruments.\n\ntaken\n\nprogram\n\nin the\n\nA link from the SIMBIOS\nWeb Page to the AERONET\narchival system allows the ocean color\ncommunity\nto access either the raw, or processed,\ndata via internet for examination,\nanalysis\nand/or\nreprocessing,\nas needed.\nAlternatively\na user may connect directly\nto the AERONET\nweb page:\naeronet.gsfc.nasa.gov:8080.\nThe algorithms,\ninputs, corrections,\nand models used in computing\nthe aerosol optical thickness,\nprecipitable\nwater (Pw), spectral irradiance, and sky radiance inversions are referenced\nin Table 14.3.
