The resulting data images usually contain the sun, so that the measurement\ngeometry can be determined accurately and unambiguously.\nAlso images can be checked\nfor cloud\ncontamination\nand other measurement\nartifacts more easily than can be done with data from scanning\nsystems. The limitation of camera systems is that the dynamic range of the whole scene must be contained\nin each image. Therefore, the camera system must have large dynamic range and some method must be\nused to attenuate the direct sunlight before it strikes the imaging optics. To get a complete sky radiance\ndistribution, including the solar aureole, it may be necessary to have an auxiliary system to measure the sky\nradiance near the sun (Ritter and Voss, 2000).\nIn addition, a sky radiance system, fitted with polarizers, can measure the Stokes parameters dealing\nwith linear polarization (Voss and Liu, 1997). These additional parameters are useful for investigating the\npolarization properties of the atmospheric aerosols, and improving the aerosol optical models.\nOne of the most important areas of the sky radiance distribution to measure is the area near the\nhorizon, opposite the sun, in the principal plane (the plane containing the sun and the zenith direction).\nThis portion of the sky contains information on the large scattering angle portion of the atmospheric aerosol\nphase function, and is very important for determining the aerosol optical properties relevant to atmospheric\ncorrection for ocean color satellites.\nThe second very important region of the sky is the solar aureole, the region near the sun. Because the\naerosol scattering phase function is strongly peaked in the forward direction, information in this region is\nimportant in determining\nthe aerosol single scattering albedo. Techniques for converting\nsky radiance\nmeasurements\nto aerosol properties have been described in Wang and Gordon (1993), Gordon and Zhang\n(1995) and Zhang and Gordon (1997a, b).\nAn example of a camera system for sky radiance distribution is described in Voss and Zibordi (1989).\nThe system described has been upgraded, for greater dynamic range, with a cooled CCD array. The basic\nsystem consists of a fisheye lens, a spectral/polarization\nfilter changer, and a digital camera. To block direct\nsunlight from hitting the array, an occulter is manually adjusted to shadow the fish-eye lens. The size of the\nocculter is approximately\n+ 20 \xc2\xb0 of the almucantar when the sun is at 60 \xc2\xb0 zenith angle; the effect of the\nocculter is obvious in data images shown in Liu and Voss (1997). Four spectral filters select the wavelength\n\n216\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite\nOptics\nFor\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nrange to be measured. Polarization\nfilters are used to collect 3 planes of polarization\nimages can be combined to determine the linear polarization stokes vectors.\n\nMeasurement\n\nin data images.\n\nThese\n\nProtocols\n\nObviously, the t\'u-st order requirement is that the field of view of the camera system be as unobstructed\nas possible, and that the measurement\nsite be located in an appropriate place with respect to the ships stack\nexhaust. If the whole field of view cannot be clear (as is usually the case), then one should try for a clear\nhemisphere,\n\nwhere\n\ndata between\n\nobstructions\n\nin the other hemisphere\n\ncan be used\n\nfor checking\n\nthe sky\n\nsymmetry.\nAs the desired objective is to derive the aerosol scattering parameters,\nthe sky must also be cloud free.\nClouds cause two problems. The fwst is easy to detect and is the direct effect of having the bright cloud in\nthe scene (in particular\non the almucantar or principle plane). Almost any cloud will overwhelm\nthe effect\nof aerosols in determining\nthe sky radiance. This effect of clouds is usually quite evident in the sky\nradiance image. The second problem is the indirect effect of clouds, while not directly causing a problem,\nshadowing\naerosols and reducing\nthe skylight caused by aerosol scattering.\nThis second effect is more\ndifficult to handle and places a more stringent requirement\non the state of cloudiness during a measurement\nsequence. This effect can often be quite visible when the atmospheric\naerosol loading is high, causing light\nbeams to be evident in the aerosol layer. For these reasons, measurements\nwith clouds present should be\navoided\n\nif at all possible.\n\nThe maximum\n\nscattering\n\nangles\n\nexisting\n\nin the sky radiance\n\ndistribution\n\noccur\n\nnear the horizon\n\nin the\n\nprinciple plane opposite the sun. For a given solar zenith angle, the maximum scattering angle is given by\nadding \xe2\x80\xa2/2 to the solar zenith angle. Since knowledge of the aerosol phase function at large scattering\nangles is important for the atmospheric\ncorrection process, measurements\nof the skY radiance distribution\nshould be taken when the sun is at large zenith angles. The optimum angle- isa compromise\nbetween getting\nlarge scattering angles and working too close to the horizon where multiple scattering effects are large\n(because of long optical paths through the atmosphere).\nA solar zenith angle of 600 has been chosen as\noptimum,\n\nbecause\n\nConcurrent\n\nof these constraints.\nwith the sky radiance\n\nmeasurements,\n\nit is important\n\nto measure\n\nthe aerosol\n\nBy combining the aerosol optical depth and skY radiance distribution,\nthe aerosol scattering\nbe determined,\ntogether with the single scattering albedo of the aerosols (Wang and Gordon,\nand Zhang, 1995; Zhang and Gordon, 1997a).\n\nData Analysis\n\noptical\n\ndepth.\n\nproperties can\n1993; Gordon\n\nProtocols\n\nData reduction of the sky radiance data is very slralghtforward,\nand is described in Voss and Zibordi\n(1989).\nBasically\nwith camera images, the data reduction process consists of simple image processing.\nEach image is multiplied\nby an absolute calibration factor and by an image that corrects for camera lens\nroll-off. This last factor is very important with a fisheye lens, as the important portion of the image is near\nthe edge where the roll-off can become very significant.\nOnce the image has been converted to radiometric\ndata, specific areas can be selected for further analysis. In particular the almucantar and principal plane can\neasily be extracted for use in inversion routines.\nReduction of the sky radiance data to get the polarization\nproperties is slightly more complicated.\nThe\ncurrent method is described in Voss and Liu (1997). Basically the Mueller matrix of the camera system is\ndescribed\nas interacting\nwith the Stokes vector of the skylight. There are three orientations\nof a linear\npolarizer in the system providing three separate Mueller matrices describing the camera system. For each\nsky direction (a pixel in the camera images)_ these Mueller matrices and the resultartt intensities measured\nby the camera form a set of simultaneous\nequations with the unknowns being the sky Stokes vectors. For\neach pixel, these equations are inverted to obtain the Stokes vector of the skylight. While these images have\nbeen evaluated\nqualitatively\n(Liu and Voss, 1997), work is currently being done to do more quantitative\ninversions following the methods of Zhang and Gordon (1997b).\n\n217\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite Ocean\nOptics\nFor\n14.3 HAND-HELD\n\nColor Sensor Validation\n\nSUN PHOTOMETERS\n\nThese instruments offer the simplest and most cost-effective\nmeans to collect data on aerosol optical\nthickness at sea. They are based on the measurement\nof the solar beam intensity, and therefore, the direct\natmospheric\ntransmittance.\nFrom this transmittance,\nafter proper\ncorrection\nfor attenuation\nby air\nmolecules, the aerosol optical thickness may be obtained (Equation 15.1). The technique is straightforward\nin principle.\nIt is difficult for an observer to point the photometer\nat the sun accurately from a moving\nplatform, but this difficulty is obviated with modern-day\ninstruments. The interest of these instruments\nalso\nresides in the fact that, in most of the oceans, aerosol optical thickness measurements\nat the time of satellite\noverpass are sufficient to verify the atmospheric\ncorrection of ocean color (Schwindling\net aL 1998). They\nallow one to estimate,\nvia the Angstrom\ncoefficient,\nthe "pseudo" phase function of the aerosols (the\nproduct of the single-scattering\nalbedo and the phase function), a key atmospheric correction variable.\nMany types of sun photometers\nhave been built and are available commercially.\nIn the following,\nwe\nfocus on two instruments,\nthe MicroTops\nsun photometer,\nmanufactured\nby Solar Light, Inc., and the\nSIMBAD radiometer,\nbuilt by the University of Lille.\nThe NASA SIMBIOS\nProgram\nmaintains a set of these instruments\nfor use during\nevaluation cruises. The objective is to collect accurate aerosol optical thickness measurements\nship cruises for comparison with values derived from satellite algorithms.\n\nocean-color\nduring the\n\na) MicroTops\nThe Solar Light, Inc. MicroTops sun photometer is a hand held radiometer used by many investigators\nthroughout the world. The popularity of MicroTops sun photometers\nis due to their ease of use, portability,\nand relatively\nlow cost.\nThe instruments\nhave five channels\nwhose wavelengths\ncan be selected by\ninterference\nfilters.\nIn order to follow the specifications\ngiven by the World Meteorological\nOrganization\n(WMO), the wavelengths\nare typically chosen at 440, 500, 675, 870 rim, with an additional channel at 940\nnm to derive integrated\nwater vapor amounts.\nIf an additional sun photometer\nis available, then it is also\ndesirable to make measurements\nat 380 and 1020 rim.\nThe MicroTops sun photometers\nuse photodiode\ndetectors coupled with amplifiers and A/D converters.\nThe collimators\nare mounted in a cast aluminum block with a 2.5 \xc2\xb0 full field of view. The MicroTops\nsun\nphotometer\nhas built-in pressure and temperature\nsensors and allows for a GPS connection to obtain the\nposition and time. A built in microprocessor\ncan calculate the aerosol optical depth, integrated water vapor,\nand air mass in real time and display these values on a LCD screen. Frequency of measurements\nis around\n3Hz. Temperature\neffects are corrected by taking dark count measurements\nwith the lid covered on startup.\nFurther information\non MicroTops sun-photometers\ncan be found in Morys (1998).\n\nb) SIMBAD\nThe SIMBAD radiometer\nwas designed by the University\nof Lille to measure both aerosol optical\nthickness\nand diffuse marine reflectance,\nthe basic atmospheric\ncorrection\nvariables.\nThe radiometric\nmeasurements\nare made in 5 spectral bands centered at 443, 490, 560, 670, and 870 nm. The ocean surface\nand the sun are viewed sequentially.\nThe same 3 \xc2\xb0 field-of-view\noptics, interference filters, and detectors are\nused in both ocean and sun viewing modes. A different electronic\ngain, low and high, is used for each\nmode, respectively.\nA specific mode allows measurement\nof the dark current. The optics are fitted with a\nvertical polarizer to reduce reflected skylight when the instrument\nis operated in ocean-viewing\nmode\n(Fougnie et al., 1999). The polarizer does not affect the sun intensity measurements,\nbecause direct solar\nradiation is not polarized.\nA GPS unit is attached to the instrument for automatic acquisition of geographic location at the time\nof measurement.\nAlso acquired automatically\nare pressure, temperature,\nand view angles. Frequency\nof\nmeasurements\nis 10 Hz. In sun-viewing\nmode, only the highest intensity measured over one second is kept\nto avoid sun-pointing\nerrors on a moving platform. Data is stored internally and downloaded\nonto diskette\nat the end of the day, or cruise. The instrument is powered with batteries, allowing 6 hours of continuous\nuse. In normal use during a cruise (see below), the internal memory and batteries allow for 3 months of\noperations without downloading\ndata or recharging the batteries.\n\n218\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite\nOptics\nFor\nInstallation\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nand Maintenance\n\nThe MicroTops\nand SIMBAD instruments need to be pointed at the sun manually. The sun is correctly\naligned when its image appears in the cross hair on a small screen (MicroTops)\nor on a target (SIMBAD).\nAfter 10-20 minutes of practice the user will become familiar with the pointing procedure and the process\nwill become second nature. It is important to get familiar with this pointing procedure on land as ship based\nmeasurements\nrequire more skill.\nThe exterior of the instrument lenses can accumulate\nsalt spray and should be inspected and cleaned if\nneeded. For the open ocean, salt is the primary contaminant.\nUnder these conditions,\na lens tissue can be\nwet with clean (filtered if possible) water or ethanol and used to remove the salt, then a dry lens tissue used\nto remove remaining water drops.\nFaulty electronics\npose a potential problem that is not always easy to detect when using MicroTops\ninstruments.\nIn the past it has been found that a leaky capacitor lowered the power and created erratic\nbehavior\nfor the shorter wavelengths\nwhere more gain is required. One can also get some idea of the\ninstrument\nstability\nby taking numerous\nmeasurements\nwith the lid covered. The voltage on all five\nchannels should be less than +__.03 inV. If the values are greater than this the unit should be sent back to\n0\nthe manufacturer\nfor repair. Voltage variability will give some idea of the noise present in the photometer.\n\nMeasurement\n\nProtocols\n\nDuring stable conditions\n(land or calm seas) pointing the radiometers\nat the sun is straightforward\nand\nmost of the measurements\nwill be accurate. Under rough ocean conditions, pointing at the sun can become\nthe major source of uncertainty,\nwith many of the measurements\nbeing off the sun. The measurements\nthat\nare off the sun wR1 have higher apparent aerosol optical depths, artifacts that bias the average positively.\nFor data acquired under rough sea conditions, repeated measurements\nof aerosol optical depths are typically\ndistributed in a comet shaped pattern, with a cluster of lower values and a tail extending to higher values. In\nthese cases, the smaller optical depth values are more accurate and the larger values, which are likely due to\npointing error, must be removed in post processing.\nSince many measurements\nmay be discarded\nin post\nprocessing, it is suggested that 25 or more measurements\nshould be made within a short period of time (less\nthan 5 minutes).\nIn general, the SIMBAD instrument is used alternatively\nin sun- and ocean-viewing\nmode. The sun\nintensity measurements\nalso allow one to compute down-welled\nsolar irradiance\naccurately\nin clear sky\nconditions,\nor when the sky is partly cloudy (<30%) with the sun not obscured by clouds. The modeled\nvalues of solar irradiance are used to normalize water-leaving\nradiance measurements.\nThe recommended\nprotocol is to make consecutively\none "dark" measurement,\nthree measurements\nin\nsun-viewing\nmode, one "dark" measurement,\nthree measurements\nin ocean-viewing\nmode, one "dark"\nmeasurement,\nthree measurements\nin sun-viewing mode, and one "dark" measurement.\nIt requires about 15\nminutes to collect a complete data set (ocean, sun, optical zero), including deploying\nthe instrument\nand\nlogging\n\nancillary\n\ndata (wind speed, sea state, cloud cover, etc.).\n\nThe current protocol (K. Knobelspiesse,\nPers. Comm.) is to set the MicroTops to internally record the\nhighest of 20 sequential measurements,\nand repeat the measurement\nat least 15 times. This procedure\nwill\nallow measurements\nmade when the instrument was not pointed accurately\nat the sun to be filtered and\nremoved\n\nduring post-processing.\n\nThe Microtops\nsun photometer\nperforms dark current measurements\nwhen it is first turned on. If the\ninstrument\'s\ntemperature\nchanges\nsince this dark current measurement,\nthe calculated\naerosol optical\nthickness value can be incorrect. (porter et al. 2000). Exposure to the sun for only a few minutes can have\nan effect, so the MicroTops\nshould be turned off and on frequently. It is recommended\nthat the MicroTops\nbe shut off and on every ten seconds when making measurements,\nor after every five continuous\nmeasurements.\nTemperature\nvariations should not be a problem for the SIMBAD, since the measurement\nprotocol\n\ninvolves\n\ngathering\n\ndark current\n\nmeasurements\n\nbefore and after in situ measurements.\n\nOn several instances we have found condensation\nto be a problem when radiometers\nwere stored in an\nair-conditioned\nroom prior to making measurements\nin the humid marine atmosphere.\nCondensation\nmay\noccur outside the SIMBAD radiometer,\nbut can also occur inside the MicroTops\n(i.e. it is not always\n\n219\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite\nOptics\nFor\n\npossible to wipe it off). This problem\nduring measurement.\n\ncan be avoided\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nby storing\n\nthe instrument\n\nat temperatures\n\nexpected\n\nFor the MicroTops\nsun photometer,\nthe latitude,\nlongitude\nand time can be set manually,\nor by\nconnecting the GPS unit directly to the radiometer.\nUsing either method, the time can be set to within one\nsecond of GMT. The latitude and longitude can also be stored in the MicroTops\nfor measurements\nat fixed\nsites. The embedded\nGPS unit in the SIMBAD automatically\nacquires the geographic location and time at\nthe beginning of each acquisition in the dark-current,\nsun-viewing,\nand ocean-viewing\nmodes.\nIn order to maintain\nthe quality of the aerosol optical thickness\nmeasurements,\nthe procedures\nsuggested\nabove should be followed and the radiometers\nshould be calibrated at least twice a year (more\nfrequently\nif the calibration site is not stable - see Chapter 7). When possible, it is also advisable to make\nmeasurements\nwith two types of instruments. This redundancy\nwill help to determine\nif any problems\nare\noccurring.\n\nData Analysis\nSeveral types of external data need to be gathered to before the conversion from instrument voltages to\naerosol optical thickness values. The air mass, M, must be calculated\nfor the location and time of the\nmeasurement.\nM is a function of the solar zenith angle, and is determined\nas shown in equation 14.3. The\noptical depth values due to water vapor,\nand removed from the signal. Calculation\n\nozone, trace gas and molecular scattering must all be determined\nof these values is described in detail in section 14.5.\n\nOnce AOT values have been calculated,\npoints representing\nerroneous\nmeasurements\nmust be\nremoved.\nIt can be difficult to point a hand held sun photometer\nat the sun accurately\nfrom a moving\nplatform, like a boat at sea Measurements\nthat were taken when the instrument wasn\'t pointed at the sun\nwill produce erroneous\nAOT values. A poorly pointed instrument\nmistakenly records less than the full\ndirect solar radiance, so the computed AOT is much higher than reality. This is a significant problem with\nthe Microtops II sun photometer,\nas its measurement\ncycle of about 3Hz is not always fast enough to avoid\nship motion (Porter et al. 2000). This is less of a problem with the SIMBAD, as its measurement\ncycle is\nabout 10Hz.\nErroneous\n\nmeasurements\n\nfrom the SIMBAD\n\nare removed\n\nmanually\n\nby plotting\n\nthe data and removing\n\nlarge values that are not part of a systematic trend. Poor pointing artifacts will appear as noise, while real\naerosol variations\nwill have a more systematic behavior\nwhen plotted as a short time series. This visual\ninspection\nand removal of large values is done for each channel, as erroneous measurement\ncan be made in\none channel that do not affect another channel. In this process, a final optical depth variability of 20% of\nthe final average value or 0.025 may be permitted when the optical depths are below 0.08. This approach\nmay slightly bias the data to lower values but it will remove the unrealistic larger values that would occur if\nthe data were not filtered.\nTwo steps were taken to reduce the possibility\nof recording\nerroneous\nmeasurements\nwith\nMicrotops.\nFirst, the measurement\nprotocol was changed. Unlike the default protocol, which saves\naverage of the 4 largest (out of 32) voltage values, the new protocol logs the largest single value of\nmeasurements.\nThis has several advantages. The largest voltage is the only value recorded, so the chance\n\nthe\nthe\n20\nof\n\nkeeping a contaminated\npoint is minimized. In addition, the total time needed to make this measurement\nis\nsmaller than with the default protocol, so more measurements\ncan be taken in a short period of time. After\nthe experiment,\na post-processing\nalgorithm is applied. This algorithm calculates the coefficient of variation\n(standard deviation divided by the mean, or CoV) for each set of measurements\nin each band. If the CoV is\nabove an arbitrary threshold of 0.05, the lowest voltage (highest AOT) value is removed,\nand the CoV\nrecalculated.\nThis is repeated until it is less than 0.05 or there are not enough points left to calculate the\nstandard deviation. The passed points are those that passed this iterative process in all bands.\n\n14.4 FAST-ROTATING\n\nSHADOW-BAND\n\nRADIOMETERS\n\nAn estimate of za can be made from calibrated measurements\nnormal incidence when there are no clouds in front of the solar\n\n220\n\nof the solar beam irradiance, E_(3.), at\ndisk. Two sun photometer\ndesigns are\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\ncommonly used to measure EN(;_): a narrow-beam\ndetector mechanically\npointed at the solar disk and a\nwide-field-of-view\nradiometer\nwith a solar occulting apparatus. The first type of sun photometer requires\ncareful angular positioning\nand can provide additional information about the forward scattering phase\nfunctions that help characterize the aerosol constituents.\nIn contrast, a radiometer equipped with an\nocculting\napparatus, known as a shadow-band\nradiometer,\nmeasures\nthe diffuse and global (upper\nhemispheric) irradiance and computes E_(3.) from the difference between the two. The device gets its name\nfrom the hemispherical metal strip that rotates around the detector and blocks the direct solar beam to yield\na signal that is from the sky only (after the effect of the arm is included).\nThe multiple wavelength\ninterference-filter-photodiode\n\nrotating shadow-band\nradiometer\n(Harrison et al., 1994) uses independent\ndetectors and an automated rotating shadow-band\ntechnique\nto make\n\nspatially resolved measurements\nat seven wavelength pass-bands. The uncertainty of the direct-normal\nspectral irradiance measurement\nmade with this type of sun photometer is comparable with that made by\nnarrow-beam\ntracking devices. A significant advantage of the shadow-band technique is that the global and\ndiffuse irradiance measurements can be used to study the solar radiation budgets and the fractional cloud\ncover at the time of the measurement.\nThe latter capability is particularly important for satellite validation\nstudies.\nIn the SIMBIOS context, direct solar and diffuse sky irradiances are critical terms for correcting\ndown-looking\nin-water radiometers\nfor self shading (Gordon and Ding i995).\nA marine versio n of the multiple-wavelength\nrotating shadow-band radiometer\nhas been developed at\nthe Brooldaaven National Laboratory (\'BNL). The BNL marine version uses a slightly modified version of\nthe detector used for continental applications. It has seven channels: one broadband silicon detector and six\nten-rim-wide channels at 415, 500, 610, 660, 870, and 940 rim. Modifications to the detector circuitry used\nfor continental applications are necessary, because the response time of the original circuitry is too slow for\nuse on a moving ship. If the response time of the detector is too slow, wave action may cause the\norientation of the radiometer to change appreciably during the time the shadow-band is occulting the sun.\nThe rotation of the shadow-band\nitself must be sufficiently fast for the same reason. The marine version of\nthe shadow-band\nradiometer\nis hereafter referred to as the BNL Fast-Rotating,\nShadow-band\nRadiometer\n(FRSR).\n\nImplicit in this terminology\n\nis that the FRSR is a multi-filter\n\nor "spectral"\n\nradiometer.\n\nThe response of the silicon cell in the detector used for continental applications is faster than one\nmillisecond,\nyet the internal preamplifiers have integrating low-noise\namplifiers that slow the overall\nresponse. The response\ntime of the detector is made faster for marine applications by reducing\nthe\nmagnitude of the low-pass filter capacitors.\nLaboratory tests do not show additional noise in the\nmeasurements as a result of this modification. The processing algorithms,\nwhich incorporate pitch, roll and\nheading measurements, are key to the instrument\'s ability to derive direct-normal beam irradiance without\ngimbals and a gyro-stabilized table.\n\nInstallation\n\nand\n\nMaintenance\n\nThe installation location of the instrument on a ship must be carefully selected. Ideally, FRSRs should\nbe mounted in an exposed location as high as possible and free of nuisance shadows from other objects.\nThis is often difficult.\nRadiation\nmeasurements\non a ship always need to consider errors from the\nubiquitous\nmasts and antennas. A ship\'s communication\nantennas have highest vertical priority as do the\nrunning lights, and one must be careful of radar beams that can cause severe electronic noise.\nOnce a\nsuitable location has been found and the instrument mounted, the diffuser should be rinsed with distilled\nwater and wiped with a moistened cloth at least once per day. The FRSR is typically mounted as a part of a\nportable radiation package that includes independent\nbroadband solar and IR radiometers.\nThe glass domes\non these radiometers\nshould be rinsed with distilled water and wiped with a moistened cloth.\nCalibration\n\nis the most essential\n\nelement\n\nof a radiation\n\nmeasurement\n\nprogram.\n\nA thorough\n\nand on-\n\ngoing calibration process is required before the FRSR can make accurate radiometric\nmeasurements\nat sea.\nTo ensure accurate measurements,\nthere are two important elements\nfor FRSR measurement\nprotocol:\ncalibration\nof the instrument\ncircuitry,\nwhich includes temperature\nstabilization\nof the detector during\nmeasurements,\nand determination\nof the extra-terrestrial\nconstants. These elements are discussed in Chapter\n7.\n\n221\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nData Analysis\nThe shadow-band\nradiometer\nmust properly measure the global and diffuse h\'radiances\ndirect-beam solar irradiance is derived by the subtraction as\nEat * (_,)=\nwhere Es,()_) is the direct-beam\n\nEs(_,)-\n\nfrom which\n\nEs_ (_1,_\n\nthe\n\n(14.5)\n\nsolar incident irradiance projected onto a horizontal plane, E,($) is the\n\nglobal irradiance incident on the horizontal plane, and E_)_) is the diffuse incident irradiance from nonforward scattering. The\ncloud filter that is currently used is based on two steps: computing signal statistics over windows of periods\nof less than two hours and using these statistics to judge the quality of the observation\nunder consideration.\nIf the standard deviation of the observations\nin a two-hour moving window is less than 0.05, a subjectively\ndefined threshold,\nand the observation\nat the center of &_ewindpwis\nals \xc2\xb0 less than 0.05, the central\nobservation\nis accepted.\nThe underpinning\nof this cloud filtering technique is that r is relatively constant\nover a period of two hours, while the cloud signal is highly variable.\nThis approach has proven relatively\nsuccessful,\nalthough improvements\nin the filter are expected in the future.\n\n14.5 SIMBIOS\n\nPROJECT\n\nAOT EXTRACTION\n\nPROTOCOLS\n\nThe SIMBIOS Project is concerned\nwith ocean color satellite sensor intercomparison\nand merger for\nbiological and interdisciplinary\nstudies of the global oceans. Imagery from different ocean color sensors\ncan now be processed by a single software package using the same algorithms, adjusted by different sensor\nspectral characteristics,\nand the same ancillary meteorological\nand environmental\ndata. This capability\nenables\ncross-comparisons\nto be made between\nsimilar data products\nderived from different\nsatellite\nsensors.\nInternally consistent cross-validation\nof these products may then be approached\nby integrating\nin\nsitu measurements\nof ocean and atmospheric\nparameters.\nThe objective of these analyses is to merge the\ndata products\nfrom the different\nsatellites to provide continuity\nof ocean color information\nover long\ntemporal and large spatial scales. Atmospheric correction of satellite radiances and, in particular, estimation\nof aerosol effects on the upwelling radiance at the top of the atmosphere,\nis one of the most difficult aspects\nof satellite ocean color remote sensing.\nAnalyses comparing\naerosol properties\nobtained\nfrom in situ\nobservations\nwith those derived from satellite ocean color sensor data are essential\nelements\nin the\nvalidation of the atmospheric\ncorrection\nalgorithms.\nThe uncertainty\nsources and magnitudes\nof AOTs\ndetermined\nfrom in situ measurements\nare discussed in earlier sections of this chapter.\nWhen the in situ\n\n223\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nmeasurements\nare matched against atmospheric\nproperties\nderived using data from a satellite sensor,\nadditional uncertainties\nresult from the different viewing angles of the satellite and surface instruments,\nand from discrepancies\nin time between the satellite and in situ observations.\nIn the case of the atmosphere,\nthese uncertainties\nare considerable.\nThe uniform calibration,\nmeasurement,\ndata analysis and quality\ncontrol protocols described in this chapter are designed to minimize these overall sources of uncertainty.\n\nExtraction\n\nof in situ AOTs\n\nThe Project\nhas recendy\nimplemented\nits own correction\nstrategy for instrument\nvoltages\ncorresponding\nto AOTs. The approach ensures a uniform AOT processing for all instruments,\nmaking the\nAOTs comparable\namongst the instruments and between instruments\nand satellite sensor AOTs derived by\nmeans of the atmospheric\ncorrection.\nAlso, the method uses a consistent set of tuning variables,\nsuch as\nancillary data, concurrently\napplied for the correction of satellite radiances. Therefore,\nsome stages of the\nsatellite and in situ data processing are identical, contributing\nto increasing confidence in the match-ups.\nFirst, instrument-specific\nprocedures\nare used to retrieve sun intensity measurements,\nV(X), from\nindividual\nsun photometers.\nIn the case of the shadow-band\nradiometer,\nthe data must be processed\nto\ndetermine\ndirect beam intensity on a plane normal to the solar beam (E,quation 14.10). The following\nprocessing\nis otherwise\nuniform\nfor all instruments,\ntaking account, however,\nof the distinct spectral\nwavelengths\nused by each sensor.\nThe Beer-Lambert-Bouguer\n\nlaw (equation\n\nV(_,)=\n\nVo(_,)\n\n14.1) can be expanded\n\n* (_\n\nas\n\nf e -M(\xc2\xb0oXt\'(x)_r\xc2\xb03(t)+r\'(_)),\n\n(14.14)\n\nwhere "rR,Zo3,and \'raare the molecular (Rayleigh) and ozone and aerosol optical thickness, respectively, and\nthe other terms have been previously defined. The depth (z) dependence of the absorption coefficients\nis omitted for\nbrevity. The particle absorption coefficient may be further decomposed\nas\n\nap (_,)=a,\n\nwhere\n\na, (&)\n\nparticles,\n\nand a n (/],) are the spectral\n\nrespectively.\n\nLaboratory\npossible\n\n(_,)+%\n\nabsorption\n\nmethods\n\ncoefficients\n\nare described\n\nto further\n\n(_,),\n\nseparate\n\nof phytoplankton,\n\nfor determining\na d (/],)\n\n(15.2)\n\nnf _,\n\noperational\n\nfractions.\n\nIt is conceptually\n\ninto absorption\n\npigmented\nseparately\n\norganic and inorganic particles, but at present, there are no well established\ndetermining\nthe absorption coefficient for inorganic particles.\n\nand de-pigmented\nestimates\n\nfractions\n\nof these\n\ndue to de-\n\nprotocols\n\nfor\n\nTo interpret\naquatic spectral reflectance\nand better understand\nphotochemical\nand photobiological\nprocesses in natural waters, it is essential to quantify the contributions\nof the individual constituents\nto the\ntotal absorption\ncoefficients\nin the ultraviolet\n(UV) and visible region of the spectrum.\nThe protocols\npresented\nhere are based on the evolution, starting with articles by Kalle (1938) and Yentsch (1962), of\nmethods for analyzing\nthe absorption\nby soluble and particulate\nmaterial in natural waters.\nLaboratory\nmeasurements\nand data analysis protocols\nare described\nfor separating\nthe total spectral\nabsorption\ncoefficient,\nfiltration\n\na (_),\nof discrete\n\nThe spectral\nspectrophotometer,\nOD(_.)\n\ninto its components\n\nby spectrophotometric\n\nmeasurements\n\nof samples\n\nprepared\n\nfrom\n\nwater samples.\nabsorbance\nof the\nare\nexpressed\n\n= Log t0[Vo ( _-)] - Log ,0[Vt ( \xc2\xa3 )].\n\nfilters and\nin\nunits\nVo (_\')is\n\nfiltrate\nfrom\nof\nOptical\nthe spectrometer\n\n231\n\nthese samples,\nas\nDensity\n(OD),\n\nmeasured\nin a\ndefined\nas\n\nresponse\n\nflux transmitted\n\nfor spectral\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nthrough\n\nthe reference\n\nmaterial\n\nand Vt (_)\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nis the response\n\nfor spectral\n\nflux transmitted\n\nthrough\n\nthe sample.\n\nFor the methods presented here the reference is either a properly hydrated GF/F blank filter ;br particle\nabsorption,\nor a clean quartz glass optical cuvette filled directly from a purified water source "or soluble\nmaterial absorption.\nNote that OD is a dimensionless\nquantity.\nThe use of base-10 logaritams\nin this\ncontext is a carryover\nfrom common\npractice in chemical\nspectroscopy\nand is the typical output of\ncommercial\nspectrophotometers\nroutinely used for these methods.\nTherefore, it is necessary to convert the\nOD measurements\ndescribed\nin this chapter to the base-e representation\nof absorbance,\ni.e. to multiply\nOD by 2.303, to conform to the convention used throughout the ocean optics protocols.\nIn general, these\nprotocols are written assuming that the instrument that is used directly\nsample relative to the appropriate reference sample.\n\ncomputes\n\nthe optical\n\ndensity\n\nof the\n\nThere has been considerable\nresearch to develop robust protocols\nthat provide the most accurate\nestimates of absorption for various material fractions in natural waters. NASA-sponsored\nworkshops\nwere\nheld at Scripps Institution\nof Oceanography\nand Bigelow Laboratory\nfor Ocean Sciences\nto review\nabsorption\nprotocols,\nevaluate\ninstrumentation,\nand define areas of consensus\nas well as areas of\nuncertainty that warrant further research (Mitchell et al. 2000).\nThe most widely used approach for estimating\nabsorption\nby particulate\nmatter in water samples\ninvolves analysis of the particles concentrated\non filters (Yentsch,\n1957).\nAbsorption\nof phytoplankton\nsuspensions\ndetermined\nusing procedures that capture most of the forward scattered light (Shibata, 1958)\ncan be related to the absorption\nmeasured on the filters to make quantitative\ncorrections for the pathlength\namplification\n\neffect (13) caused\n\npathlength\namplification\nnomenclature\nof Butler\ncoefficient\n\nby the highly scattering\n\nfilter medium\n\n(Duntley,\n\n1942; Butler,\n\n1962).\n\nThe\n\nparameter\nwas symbolized\nas [3 by Kiefer and SooHoo (1982) following\nthe\n(1962).\nThis symbol should not to be confused\nwith the volume scattering\n\nfl(;L, _P) used in other chapters\n\nof this Technical\n\nMemorandum.\n\nKiefer and SooHoo (1982) reported a constant to scale the red peak of chlorophyll\nabsorption\nfor\nnatural particles retained on GF/C filters to the diffuse absorption coefficients\ndetermined\non suspensions\nby Kiefer et al. (1979).\nThe diffuse absorption\ncoefficient\nis double the value of the volume absorption\ncoefficient of interest here (Preisendorfer,\n1976). Mitchell and Kiefer (1984, 1988a) made direct estimates\nof volume absorption\ncoefficients for phytoplankton\nsuspensions\nand absorbance\non glass fiber filters with\nthe same particles to develop empirical equations\nthat relate the amplification\nfactor to the glass fiber\nsample optical density.\nThis procedure\nis the basis of most laboratory\nmethods for determining\nparticle\nabsorption in water samples.\nField\n\napplications\n\nof these\n\nquantitative\n\nestimates\n\nof\n\nap (_)\n\nwere\n\nreported\n\nby Mitchell\n\nand Kiefer\n\n(1984, 1988b) and Bricaud and Stramski (1990). More detailed empirical results to correct for pathlength\namplification\nwere reported\nby Mitchell (1990) for various filter types and diverse cultures coccoid\ncyanobacteria,\nnanochlrophytes,\ndiatoms, chrysophytes\nand dinoflagellates\nwith sizes ranging from 2 Ima to\n20p.m.\nCleveland\nand Weidemann\n(1993) and Tassan and Ferrari (1995) found that the empirical\nrelationships\nof Mitchell (1990) were consistent\nwith similar types of phytoplankton,\nbut Moore et al.\n(1995) reported\nlarge differences\nin the amplification\nfactor for Synechococcus\nsp. (WH8103)\nand\nProchlorococcus\nmarinus that were about half the size of the smallest cells studied by Mitchell (1990).\nSimilar results were obtained by Allali et al. (1997) for natural populations\nof the Equatorial\nPacific\ndominated\nby picoplankton.\nFor samples with substantial turbidity and scattering due to inorganic matter\n(coastal, shelf, coccolithophore\nblooms), methods to correct for resulting artifacts have been described by\nTassan and Ferrari (1995a, 1995b).\nTable 15.1 provides a summary\nof various published\nresults for\npathlength\n\namplification\n\nfactors.\n\nSeparation\nof the particle\nfraction into phytoplankton\nand other components\nis of considerable\necological\nand biogeochemical\ninterest.\nEarly efforts to separate\nabsorbing\ncomponents\nfor natural\nparticles included treatment with organic solvents, UV radiation, and potassium permanganate\n(references\ncan be found in Shifrin, 1988, and Bricaud and Stramski, 1990). The most widely used chemical method is\nbased on methanol extraction\n(Kishino et al. 1985, 1986).\nA recent method consists of bleaching\nthe\nphytoplankton\npigments by sodium hypochlorite\n(Tassan and Ferrari, 1995a; Ferrari and Tassan, 1999).\n\n232\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite\nOptics\nFor\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nSpectral fluorescence\nmethods to estimate the fraction of photosynthetically\nactive absorption,\nif separate\ntotal particulate absorption has been determined, have been reported by Sosik and Mitchell, (1995).\nSoluble\nabsorption\nobservations\nwere described\nby Bricaud\net al. (1981)\nfor diverse\nocean\nenvironments,\nincluding\noligotrophic\nand eutrophic regions.\nOther field reports can be found in the\nreferences\nlisted in more recent articles (Carder et al., 1989a, 1989b; Blough et aL, 1993; Vodacek et aL,\n1996; Hoge et aL, 1993; Nelson et al., 1998; D\'Sa et al., 1999).\nSpectrophotometric\nmeasurement\nof\nabsorption\nby dissolved materials is straightforward,\nbut has limits due to the very small signal for short\npathlengths\nroutinely\nemployed\n(usually\n10cm), and to difficulties\nin maintaining\nquality control of\npurified water used as a reference.\nThis chapter\ndefines protocols\nfor the operational\ndeterminations\nof absorption\ncoefficients\nfor\nparticulate\nand soluble matter in water samples.\nMethods are specified\nfor separating\nparticulate\nand\nsoluble material by filtration, partitioning\ntotal particulate\nabsorption into contributions\nby phytoplankton\nand de-pigmented\nparticles (detritus),\nand corrections\nfor pathlength\namplification\ndue to semi-diffuse\ntransmittance\nof the filters. Recommendations\nare made based on widely accepted methods and processing\nprocedures.\nNASA-sponsored\n(Mitchell et al. 2000).\n\n15.2 SAMPLE\n\nworkshops\n\nhave confirmed\n\naspects\n\nof previously\n\nreported\n\nmethods\n\nACQUISITION\n\nWater samples-should-be\ntaken using Niskin\nthe surface in-water optical measurements,\nand\nat least the top optical depth.\nWhen possible,\nthroughout the upper 300m of the water column\nfor PAR irradiance,\n\nvarious\n\nln(E(0)/E(z))=7),\n\n(or similar) bottles\nat depth increments\nsamples should be\n(or in turbid water,\n\nto provide\n\nat the site of, and simultaneously\nwith,\nsufficient to resolve variability\nwithin\nacquired at several depths distributed\nup to seven diffuse attenuation\ndepths\n\na basis for relating\n\nthe spectroscopic\n\nmeasurements\n\nof\n\nabsorption\nto in situ profile measurements.\nSamples should be drawn immediately\nfrom the in situ\nsampling bottles into clean sampling bottles using clean silicon rubber or Tygon tubing or by directly\nfilling the sample bottles from the Niskin bottle spigot. If Niskin bottles will not be sampled immediately,\nprecautions\nmust be taken to ensure large particles that settle are re-suspended.\nThis can be done by\ntransferring all water from the Niskin to a bottle or carboy larger than the total volume of the Niskin so that\nthe entire water sample can be mixed (invert bottle numerous times to mix by turbulence),\nor by draining a\nsmall amount of water from the Niskin and manually inverting the entire Niskin prior to sub-sampling.\nSample bottles should be kept cool (ideally near in situ temperatures),\nand dark prior to sample\npreparations.\nPreparations\nshould be completed as soon as possible after sampling, but no later than several\nhours after the sample was acquired.\n\n15.3 SPECTROPHOTOMETER\n\nCHARACTERISTICS\n\nAND\n\nCALIBRATION\nA spectrophotometer\nused for absorption measurements\nfollowing\nchapter must meet the following minimum performance\nspecifications:\n\nthe protocols\n\npresented\n\nin this\n\n1.\n\nThe unit\'s monochromator,\nor spectrograph, must yield a Full-Width at Half-Maximum\n(FWHM)\nbandwidth\n<-4 nm. Most modern spectrophotometers,\nwhether single or double beam, automatically\nstore\nthe instrument\'s\nreference spectrum and recorded sample speclTa are automatically\ncorrected\nto yield\nODfp(g)\n\nrelative\n\nto the reference\n\nblank filter.\n\nA new\n\ninstrument\n?\n\nreference\n\nbaseline\n\nscan\n\nshould\n\nbe\n\nmeasured\neach time the spectrophotometer\nis powered up, and whenever\nits configuration\nhas been\nchanged.\nThe baseline should also be checked regularly (every 1 hr to 2 hr) during extended\nperiods of\nanalysis.\nFrequency of baseline verification\nwill depend on the performance\nand stability of each\ninstrument and should be determined by the investigator prior to executing routine work.\nUncorrected\nbaseline drift, and changes in sorting filters or lamp source can cause systematic measurement anomalies.\nWavelength accuracy and measurement\nprecision should also be checked during the analyses (Sect. 15.3\nabove).\nb. Spectrophotometric\n\nMeasurement\n\nProcedure\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nWarm up the spectrophotometer\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMeasure\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIf using frozen samples,\ndishes on FSW to ensure\nrefrigerate\n\nthe initial instrument\n\nfor 30 minutes.\nbaseline\n\nand wavelength\n\ncalibration.\n\nremove the filters from the storage container\nand place them in petri\nhydration.\nAllow the samples to thaw for approximately\n5 rain and then\n\nthem in the dark until each filter is ready for analysis.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAn instrument-specific\nsample-mounting\ndevice is recommended\nto hold filters against a quartz\nglass mounting plate. These mounts should be secure when placed in the sample compartment\nand hold the sample perpendicular\nto the illumination\nbeam so only the filter and the quartz plate\nare in the beam. Usually, these mounts must be custom fabricated specifically\nfor each different\ninstrument.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nClean the quartz faceplates of the mounting device with purified water and detergent\nRinse them with purified water and ethanol, and dry them thoroughly using lint-free\ntissues.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSet the appropriate\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMount two pre-soaked\nreference beam).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nTo test for proper filter hydration, confnun that there is a drop of FSW left on the mounting plate\nwhen the filter is lifted. With the filter on the mounting plate there should be a slight sheen on the\ntop surface of the filter, and a very narrow (~ 1 ram) border of water around the edges of the filter.\nBe careful not to use too much water, or the sample may wash away.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nExamine\nthe back of the filter on the mounting plate to be sure that no bubbles are trapped\nbetween the filter and the quartz glass plate on the sample holder. There should be a uniform layer\nof water between the filter and quartz glass mounting plate. If bubbles are present, which will be\nobvious, pick up the filter with forceps, and replace it on the plate with a slight dragging motion\nacross a drop of filtered seawater.\nRe-inspect\nthe back of the filter and repeat the foregoing\nprocedure until no bubbles are present. Adjust the amount of FSW as necessary to ensure proper\nhydration.\n\ninstrument\n\nparameters\n\nand water saturated\n\naccording\n\nto the manufacturer\'s\n\nblank filters (one for the sample\n\n237\n\nif needed.\nlaboratory\n\ninstructions.\nbeam, and one for the\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\nForSatellite Color\nOcean Sensor\nValidation\nAlternative ountshatexposeothsides thefilter to air maybeused avoidbubbles\nm\nt\nb\nof\nto\naltogether.\nSampleydration\nh\nismore\ndifficulttomaintain hen\nw using\nthistypeoffiltermount o\ns\ntheinvestigator develop satisfactory\nmust\na\nprocedure\ntoensureroper\np\nhydrationf samplend\no\na\nreference.\nRuntheinstrument\nbaselineorrection thetwoblanks.Formost ommercial\nc\nusing\nc\nunits,this\nbaseline ill beautomatically asthereference\nw\nused\ntocalculateDfp(;_). If using a quartz plate, check the reference filter\nafter every 5-6 scans, and hydrate as needed.\nIf the filters are mounted in air, hydrate the blank\nbefore every scan.\n\nSample\n\nFilter Preparation\n\nAfter\n\npreparing\n\nspectrophotometer,\nshape\n\nof the\n\nan\n\nfor De-pigmented\n\nap(Z)\n\nthe sample\n\na d (/1,)\n\nspectrum\n\nfilter\n\nshould\nusually\n\nParticle\nsample\n\nbe processed\ndecreases\n\nAbsorption\nand\n\ndetermining\n\nto remove\nmonotonically\n\nits\n\nits pigments\n\nOD_(2)\n\nspectrum\n\nand determine\n\nwith wavelength,\n\nfollowing\n\non\n\na a (_,).\n\nthe\nThe\n\nexponential\n\nform that is flatter than the shape of the soluble absorption spectrum.\nSince the goal is generally to get an\nestimate of phytoplankton\nabsorption,\nif there is a residual chlorophyll\na absorption\npeak in the red near\n675 ran, the extraction\nprocess should be repeated to remove it. Variations of this method include use of\nhot or boiling methanol and varying extraction times. Use of hot methanol has risks due to flammability,\nand volatility.\nIf this process is used, extra precautions\nmust be taken.\nBleaching\nof the organic pigments can also be accomplished\nfor situations with difficult to extract\npigments including phycobilins\nor other chemically polar pigments that do not extract well in methanol.\nPigment extraction\nin a chemical solvent, such as methanol, is a fundamentally\ndifferent chemical process\nthan bleaching\nthe pigments using sodium hypochlorite\n(NaCIO).\nBleaching\ninvolves placing a small\namount of 0.1% active chlorine solution onto the filter, then rinsing it off with FSW. The NaC10 oxidizes\nthe pigment molecules,\nmaking their light absorption\nnegligible.\nFSW rinses then remove the excess\nNaCIO, which absorbs negligibly\nat wavelengths\n>400 nm, but absorbs strongly at shorter wavelengths.\nThe bleaching method of pigment removal has been shown to be effective in situations where methanol\ncannot be used, as on cellulose membranes\nsuch as the 0.22 lxm Millipore filter, or when phycobilins\nare\npresent (Tassan and Ferrari 1995a; Mitchell et al. 2000). This procedure can also be adapted for use with\nparticulate suspensions.\nNeither methanol\nabsorption\ninto \'algal\'\n\nextraction,\nnor NaCIO oxidation, provides an ideal means of separating\nparticulate\nand \'detrital\' components.\nIn each case, the action of the chemical agent is not well\n\n238\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite\nOptics\nFor\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nunderstood,\nand in many situations the two methods will yield very different results. The decision to apply\neither the bleaching, or methanol extraction,\nmethod will depend on the situation.\nFor example, in inland\nwaters where either cyanobacteria,\nor chlorophytes,\nare dominant, the bleaching technique is preferred,\nbecause of the presence\nof phycobilins\nand of extraction\nresistant algae (e.g. Porra 1990).\nIn coastal\noceanic waters, on the other hand, the methanol\ntechnique\nis preferred,\nbecause the results will be\ncomparable\nto previously\npublished results and there is no particular advantage to using bleach.\nIn openocean samples (e.g. the Sargasso Sea), however, absorption\nby phycobilins\nis small, but present in some\nparticulate\nabsorption\nsamples and in methanol-extracted\nfilters (N.B. Nelson unpublished\ndata).\nThe\nmethanol technique\nwill provide results which are comparable\nto earlier studies, but with errors due to\nincomplete extraction and wavelength shifts in the phycobilin absorption bands.\n\na. used\n\nif the methanol\n\nwith FSW (or fresh water\n\nPREPARATION\nto determine\n\nAND\n\nag (3,),\n\nthe\n\nspectral\n\nabsorption\ncoefficient spectrum of gelbstoff, often referred to as dissolved organic matter (CDOM).\nWater\nsamples are collected and particulate\nmaterial is removed by filtration.\nThe absorption\nof the filtrate is\nmeasured, relative to purified water, using a spectrophotometer.\nAll equipment utilized to prepare soluble\nabsorption\nsamples must minimize contamination\nby organic, or otherwise colored, material. Samples must\nbe protected\nfrom photo-degradation\nduring preparation\nand measurements.\nPlastic or glass filtration\napparatus may be used, provided that the units are equipped with mesh filter supports made either of\nstainless steel or plastic, and not with ground glass flits. Glass flits tend to become clogged over time, and\nmay cause uneven distribution on the filter, reduce the rate of filtration and may contaminate the sample\nfiltrate.\nMembrane\nfilters with 0.2 ltm pore size (e.g., Nuclepore TM polycarbonate\nfilters)\nare\nrecommended for this procedure. The membrane filters should be pre-soaked in 10% HC1, rinsed with 75100 mL of freshly purified water, and rinsed again with a 75 - 100 mL of the sample before it is used.\nTests with purified water have shown that all filters leach contamination that resembles soluble absorption\n(data not shown).\nUsing polycarbonate membrane filters, an acid soak, pure water rinse and sample rinse\nminimizes\nthis contamination.\nStill, we have found the sample preparation procedure increases the\napparent absorption spectra of purified water that is prepared as though it were a sample when referenced to\npurified water drawn directly into the measuring cuvette from the pure water system. Therefore correction\nfor this sample preparation blank is recommended.\nGlass fiber filters should be avoided if possible because they have been shown to cause rather\nsevere contamination of the filtrate in tests using purified water. For samples collected from very turbid\nwaters, glass fiber filters have routinely been used as a pre-filter to minimize clogging of the final filtration\nwith a membrane filter (Kowalczuk,\n1999).\nIn such cases the investigator must develop a procedure to\nrinse the glass fiber filter to ensure that the contamination from this method is minimized. Since situations\nrequiring pre-fiitration often coincide with large soluble absorption coefficients, the effects may be easily\ncorrected but it is the responsibility of the investigator to demonstrate this.\nCareful assessment\nof the\ncontamination\nof any method, and proper corrections must be carried out and reported.\nPreviously\nwe recommended\nthe use of amber-colored\nborosilicate\nglass bottles (e.g. Qorpak TM\nbottles), that screen ambient light, for sample preparation and to store laboratory prepared standard water.\nHowever, recent work (details not shown) indicate that the amber bottles may leach some colored material\ninto the purified standard water that is prepared before cruises and used to assess the quality of purified\nwater prepared\n\nat sea.\n\nTherefore\n\nwe now recommend\n\n240\n\nuse of clear borosilicate\n\nQorpak\n\nTM\n\nbottles\n\n(or\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nequivalent)\nexperiment,\n\nPurified\n\nfor sample preparations\nand for the preparation\nof the standard reference\nall filtration apparatus and storage bottles should be thoroughly cleaned.\n\nwater for soluble absorption\n\nPurified\n\nwater freshly\n\nwater.\n\nPrior\n\nto each\n\nmeasurements\n\ndrawn from a water purification\n\nsystem,\n\nsuch as the Millipore\n\nMilli-Q,\n\nMillipore\n\nAlpha-Q, and Bamstead\nNanopure units, or their equivalent,\nis strongly recommended\nfor use at sea in\npreparing pure water for absorption reference, blanks and for equipment rinses specified in these protocols.\nMitchell et al. (2000) compared the water-to-air\nbaseline reference of purified water prepared with these\nthree water purification systems.\nAll three systems provided similar results in baseline tests relative to air\nat wavelengths\nbetween 300 nm and 900 rim, while small differences were found below 300 nn_ It is also\nrecommended\nto prepare a set of standard purified water samples prior to a field deployment\nas a reference\nto check dally for pure-water\nsystem degradation,\ne.g. due to poor quality feed water. Even though bottled\npurified water standards have been found to deteriorate slightly over time, especially\nfrom 250 nm to 325\nnm, they provide invaluable quality control and an alternative source of reference water in situations when\nthe purification\nsystem performance\ndegrades dramatically.\n\nPre-cruise\n\npreparations\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSample bottles (clear borosilicate\nQorpak TM with polyethylene lined caps) used to collect sample\nfi!trate or to store standard reference Water need to be thoroughly cleaned in advance to remove\nany potential organic contaminants.\nSequential\nsoaks and rinses in dilute detergent, purified\nwater, and 10 % HC1, followed by a final copious rinse in purified water, are recommended.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRinse plastic caps with 10 % HCI, twice with freshly prepared\nMillipore Alpha-Q system), and dry them at 70 \xc2\xb0 C for 4 hr to 6 hr.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCombust bottles\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFill clean, combusted\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAssemble\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThese\n\nwith aluminum\n\nstandards\n\nprepared\n\nbottles and clean caps.\n\nare used dally\n\nwater\n\n(e.g.\n\nusing\n\na\n\nfoil covers at 450 \xc2\xb0 C for 4 hr to 6 hr.\n\nbottles with fresh purified\n\nthe combusted\n\npurified\n\nduring\n\ncruises\n\nwater drawn\n\ndirectly\n\nfrom the purification\n\nunit.\n\nStore in the dark.\nto evaluate\n\nthe quality\n\nof purified\n\nwater\n\nfreshly\n\nat sea.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThis carefully prepared standard water sometimes must be used as the reference material for actual\nsample analysis. Because the signals are small, instrument noise is a large fraction of the signal, even for\nhigh quality spectrophotometers.\nTherefore\nsubtraction\nof an individual\nblank spectrum,\nincluding\nits\n\n244\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nFor SateUite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nnoise, is strongly discouraged\nas this effectively doubles the noise of an already noisy signal. Instead, it is\nrecommended\nthat a smoothed blank be determined from many individual blank spectra provided that the\ninvestigator\ncan demonstrate,\nas in Figure 15.3B that there is consistency\namong the population\nof blank\nspectra that are determined.\nThe procedure of determining blanks at least each day during routine sampling\nprovides an important quality control on the sample preparation protocols.\nIf the blank is found to deviate\nconsiderably\nfrom the norm, the investigator\nshould immediately determine the cause of the discrepancy.\n\nb. Null point corrections\n\nto soluble absorption\n\nspectra\n\nThe absorption\nspectrum of pure water varies strongly with temperature, especially in the wavelength\nregion between 650 nm and 750 nm, but at other wavelengths\nas well (Pegau and Zaneveld,\n1993). To\navoid temperature\nrelated measurement\nartifacts, the sample and reference\nshould be maintained\nat the\nsame temperature,\nbut in practice, this is often difficult to do. If strong temperature residuals are apparent\nin the spectra near 750 nm, one must inspect the data to determine an appropriate\nwavelength\nrange to use\nas a null point. For the data in Figure 15.3, it appears that assuming a null point as the average from 590600 nm is reasonable.\nThis assumption\nmay not be reasonable in turbid lake, bay and coastal waters,\nhowever, where large soluble absorption values may persist into the near IR. Selection of wavelengths\nfor\nnull correction must be evaluated carefully for each data set, following principles discussed at more length\nby Mitchell\n\nParticle\n\net al. (2000).\n\nAbsorption\n\nTo compute\n\nCoefficients\nparticle\n\nabsorption\n\nap(_)\n\nin suspension\n\nfrom spectrophotometfic\n\nODfp(_,)\n\nwith the particles\nconcentrated\non a GF/F filter, it is necessary\nto appropriately\npathlength.\nThis includes substituting the geometric optical pathlength of the particles\nscaling\n\nfactor,\n\nsample.\n\nfl,\n\naccounting\n\nThe geometric\n\nfor the increase\n\nabsorption\n\npathlength\n\nin the optical\n\nmeasurement\n\nls of the filtered material\n\nadjust the optical\nin suspension,\nand a\n\npath by scattering\nin suspension\n\nmeasured\n\nwithin\n\nthe filter\n\nis given by\n\nls _ Vf\n--_-,\nwhere Vf is the volume\n\nof water filtered\n\n(15.4)\n\nand Af is the clearance\n\narea of the filter calculated\n\nfrom the\n\ndiameter Df of the part of each filter that contains the particles. Null point\n\ncorrections\n\nto particle\n\nabsorption\n\nspectra\n\nTo correct for residual offsets in the sample filter relative to the reference, and for scattering artifacts\ndue to particle loading, it is assumed that a null absorption\nwavelength\nin the infrared can be identified.\nHistorically,\nmany investigators\nused 750 nm as the null absorption wavelength,\nbut recent reports indicate\nthat this wavelength\nis too short for some waters. It is recommended\nthat the null wavelength be set at 800\nnm (or longer), and that the investigator\nmust examine the spectra to evaluate residual absorption structure\nnear the null wavelength.\n\nRather\n\nthan use a single wavelength,\n\na mean\n\nODfv(;_ ) in a 10 nm interval\n\n(e.g.\n\n790 nm to 800 nm) may be used as the null value to minimize the introduction\nof noise in the null\ncorrection\nprocedure.\nMitchell et aL (2000) discuss, at more length, factors affecting the choice of an\nappropriate\nwavelength\nfor estimating\nODnu n . In Case 2 waters, the definition of the null absorption\nis\nmore difficult and the investigator\nmay consider\nparticle absorption (Tassan and Ferrari, 1995a).\n\nc. We recommend\nthat the user compare several spectrophotometers\nfor raw optical density of properly\nhydrated\nsamples relative to blank filters and ensure the unit they use does not deviate from the typical\nresult\nof most systems\nfor which amplifications\nfactors\n(_) have been determined\n(Table\n15.1).\nAlternatively\none must determine the pathlength\namplification\nfor the instrument of choice, a laborious and\nunnecessary\nprocedure\nif a spectrophotometer\nis selected that does not cause the bias illustrated in Figure\n15.1.\n\nAbsorption\n\nspectra for particles\n\nAn alternative\n\nmethod,\n\ntransferred\n\ndeveloped\n\nto glass slides\n\nby Allali et al. (1995), to estimate\n\nabsorption\n\ncoefficients\n\nof cultures\n\nand seawater\nsamples is to freeze transfer the particles to transparent\nmicroscope\nslides, following\nthe\nprotocols\nof Hewes and Holm-Hansen\n(1983). The investigator\nmust have an integrating\nsphere or\nequivalent\nscattered transmission\naccessory to implement this method.\nThis procedure\nproduced results\ncomparable\nto the GF/F filter method in comparisons\nreported by Mitchell et al. (2000), but sufficient\nuncertainties\nremain that the GF/F method continues to be recommended\nfor the present.\n\nTransmission-Reflectance\n\n(T-R) Method\n\nTassan and Ferrari (1995a) described a modification\nof the light-transmission\nmethod that corrects for\nbackscattering.\nThis technique combines\nlight-transmission\n(T) and light-reflection\n(R) measurements,\ncarried out using an integrating\nsphere attached to a dual-beam spectrophotometer.\nThe data analysis is\n\n247\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nperformed by a theoretical model that eliminates the effect of light backscattering\nby the particles.\nAt the\nScripps workshop, the global error of the T-R method was comparable to the error yielded by the simpler T\nmethod for mono-cultures.\nSubsequent modifications\nof the T-R experimental\nroutine (Tassan and Ferrari,\n1998; Ferrari and Tassan, 1999) yielded a significant\nreduction of the experimental\nerror.\nTassan and\nFerrari (1995) reported that for case 1 waters that have negligible inorganic particle load, the amplification\nfactor for GF/F filters determined\nwith the T-R methods is similar to those determined\nby Mitchell (1990).\nThe T-R method is particularly\nsuited for applications\nto samples containing\nhighly scattering mineral\nparticles that are commonly found in Case 2 waters. Despite the more complicated\nprocedure including an\ninstrument with an integrating sphere, this method should be considered\nin special circumstances,\nand with\nfurther development\nmay eventually supercede the presently recommended\ntransmission\nprotocol.\n\nAbsorption\n\nspectra for seawater\n\nFor most ocean regions,\n\nfiltered\n\nthrough\n\nthe optical\n\nmembrane\n\nfilters\n\ndensity of dissolved\n\nor cartridges\n\norganic\n\nmaterial,\n\nrelative\n\nto purified\n\nwater in a\n\ntypical 10 cm pathlength\ncuvette, is very small in the 400-600 nm region of most interest to ocean color\nsatellite investigations.\nTo ensure a common frame of reference for the global data collected by diverse\ninvestigators,\n\nwe recommend\n\nwater directly\nindependent\nduring\n\nintroduced\nreference\n\nACE-Asia\n\nODr_ a (_,)\n\nto properly\n\nspectra\n\ncleaned\n\n(250-850\n\nquartz cuvettes.\n\nof the quality of the purified\n\nare shown\n\nin Figure\n\n15.2.\n\nnm) be determined\n\nwater.\n\nThe purpose\n\nODrw a (_)\n\nODrw a (_1,) should\n\nrelative\n\nto air for purified\n\nof such spectra\n\nspectra\n\nis to obtain\n\nfor the sample\n\nbe determined\n\ncuvette\n\nan\nused\n\ndaily for the sample\n\nand\n\nreference cuvettes used in analyses.\nThe investigator\nshould keep careful records of this data and assess\nany bias in final estimates that may be attributed to problems with the reference water. The recommended\nprocedure is to subtract a cruise (or global) mean\nof this blank (solid line in Figure 15.3B) from the raw sample OD values, and then to adjust this difference\nto zero at a null reference (Equation 15.3). The smoothed global blank was determined\nby taking the mean\nof all blanks for each cruise we have completed since 1998, subsequently\ntaking the mean of all cruises and\nlastly fitting an exponential\nfunction to the global mean after setting a null point as the average from 590600 nm. There can be small differences\nin blank spectra cruise to cruise, but we do not find this to be\nsignificant relative to the overall statistics of all cruises or the variance within a single cruse.\nFor relatively\nweakly absorbing\nsamples like open oceans observed\nduring ACE-Asia,\nthere is negligible\napparent\nabsorption\n> 600 nm and there is clear evidence in 3A of uncompensated\ntemperature\neffects 650-800 nm.\nTherefore\nwe chose to set the null value as the mean from 590-600 nm.
