Bidigare\n\nl Department\n2Horn\n3Center\n\nSensor Validation\n\nPoint\n\n1, Laurie Van Heukelem\n\nof Oceanography,\n\nEnvironmental\n\nfor Hydro-Optics\n\nUniversity\n\nLaboratory,\n\nand Remote\n\n2 and Charles\n\nSensing,\n\nof Hawaii,\n\nUniversity\nSan Diego\n\nC. Trees 3\nHawaii\n\nof Maryland,\nState\n\nMaryland\n\nUniversity,\n\nCalifornia\n\n16.1 INTRODUCTION\nMarine phytoplankton utilize chlorophyll a as their major light harvesting pigment for photosynthesis.\nOther accessory pigment compounds, such as chlorophylls b and c, carotenoids and phycobiliproteins,\nalso\nplay a significant role in photosynthesis\nby extending the organism\'s optical collection\nwindow, thereby\nimproving absorption efficiencies\nand adaptation capabilities.\nThe important chlorophyll\ndegradation\nproducts found in the aquatic environment\nare the chlorophyllides,\nphaeophorbides,\nand phaeophytins.\nThe\npresence, or absence, of the various photosynthetic pigments is used to separate the major algal groups, and\nto map the chemotaxonomic\ncomposition of phytoplankton in the oceans.\nThe unique optical properties of chlorophyll a have been used to develop spectrophotometric\n(Jeffrey\nand Humphrey 1975) and fluorometric\n(Holm-Hansen\net al. 1965) measurement\ntechniques.\nWith the\ncommercial\navailability of fluorometers for routine measurements\nof chlorophyll a, this pigment became a\nuniversal parameter in biological\noceanography for estimating\nphytoplankton biomass and productivity.\nThese optical methods can significantly under- or overestimate chlorophyll a concentrations, because of the\noverlap of the absorption and fluorescence\nbands of co-occurring\nchlorophylls\nb and c, chlorophyll\ndegradation products, and accessory pigments (Trees et al. 1985; Smith et al. 1987; Hoepffner\nand\nSathyendranath\n1992; Bianchi et al. 1995; Tester et al. 1995).\nThe application of HPLC to phytoplankton pigment analysis has lowered the uncertainty for measuring\nchlorophyll\na and pheopigments,\nas well as the accessory pigments, since compounds are physically\nseparated and individually\nquantified.\nH:PLC has provided oceanographers\nwith a powerful tool for\nstudying the processes affecting the phytoplankton pigment pool.\nPigment distribution is useful for\nquantitative\nassessment\nof phytoplankton\ncommunity\ncomposition,\nphytoplankton\ngrowth\nrate and\nzooplankton grazing activity.\nFor low uncertainty determinations of chlorophylls a, b, and c, chlorophyll\ndegradation products, and\ncarotenoid\npigments, I-IPLC techniques are recommended.\nIt should be noted, however, that the reversephase Cl8 HPLC method recommended by the Scientific Committee on Oceanographic\nResearch (SCOR)\n(Wright et al. 1991) is not capable of separating monovinyl chlorophyll a from divinyl chlorophyll\na, nor\nmonovinyl\nchlorophyll b from divinyl chlorophyll\nb. This method, therefore,\nonly provides estimates;\nmethods for optically resolving monovinyl chlorophyll a and divinyi chlorophyll a are given below.\nDivinyl chlorophyll\na, the major photosynthetic pigment found in Prochlorococcus,\naccounts for 10 %\nto 60 % of the total chlorophyll\na in subtropical\nand tropical oceanic waters (Goericke and Repeta 1993;\nLetelier et al. 1993; Andersen et al. 1996; Bidigare and Ondrusek\n1996; Gibb et al. 2000).\nDivinyl\nchlorophyll\na is spectrally different from normal (monovinyl)\nchlorophyll\na and its presence results in a\nsignificant\noverestimation\nof total chlorophyll\na concentration\nas determined\nby the conventional\nHPLC\nmethods (Goericke\nand Repeta 1993; Letelier et al. 1993; Latasa et al. 1996). To avoid these errors, it is\nrecommended\nthat monovinyl\nand divinyl chlorophyll\na be spectrally resolved, or chromatographically\nseparated,\nto obtain an unbiased determination\nof total chlorophyll\na for ground-truthing\nsatellite ocean\ncolor algorithms and imagery. Total chlorophyll a, TChl a, is the sum of divinyl chlorophyll\na, monovinyl\nchlorophyll\na, chlorophyllide\na, and chlorophyll\na epimers and allomers.\nThese co-eluting\nchlorophyll\nspecies can be resolved spectrally following CLs HPLC chromatography\n(Wright et al. 1991) and quantified\n\n258\n\nOcean Protocols For\nOptics\n\nSatellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nusing dichromatic\nequations\nat 436 ran and 450 nm (Latasa et al. 1996).\nAlternatively,\nchlorophyll\nspecies can be separated chromatographically\nand individually\nquantified\nusing\ntechniques (see below).\nThe protocols\nspecified below for HPLC pigment analyses follow closely those\nJGOFS Core Measurement\nProtocols (UNESCO 1994). Both sets of protocols include:\n1.\n\nUse of Whatman\n\n2.\n\nExtraction\n\n3.\n\nCalibration\n\nGF/F glass fiber filters, approximately\n\nin aqueous\n\nacetone;\n\nthese two\nCs HPLC\n\nprescribed\n\nin the\n\n0.7 grn pore size;\n\nand\n\nwith standards.\n\nThe present protocols differ from the IGOFS protocols in one critical respect.\nAbsorption\nof light in\nseawater, or any other medium, is a volumetric process, even though the volume absorption\ncoefficient may\nvary with the density of the medium. For ocean color and optical analyses, therefore, the concentrations\nin\nseawater\nof all phytoplankton\npigments shall be expressed in units of mass per unit volume of seawater\n(_tg L -l or mg m-3). This differs from the JGOFS\nof all phytoplankton\npigments should be expressed\n\nprotocols, which specify\nin ng Kg -1.\n\nthat concentrations\n\nin seawater\n\nIn addition to HPLC analyses, it is recommended\nthat the standard fluorometric methodology\nused for\nmeasuring chlorophylls\nand pheopigments\n(Holm-Hansen\net al. 1965, Stricldand and Parson 1972) also be\napplied to the same extracted\npigment samples used for HPLC analysis.\nProtocols\nfor fluorometric\nmeasurements\nof chlorophyll\na and pheopigments\nare given here in Chapter 17. For a more in depth review\nof guidelines for measuring phytoplankton\npigments in oceanography see Jeffrey et aL (1997)\n\n16.2 SAMPLING\nPIGMENTS\n\nPROTOCOLS\n\nFOR PHYTOPLANKTON\n\nWater Samples\nWater samples should\nsurface in-water upwelled\n\nbe taken using, e.g., Niskin boules at the site of, and simultaneously\nwith, the\nradiance and reflectance\nmeasurements,\nand at depth increments\nsufficient to\n\nresolve variability within at least the top optical depth. The K(z,g), profiles over this layer will be used to\ncompute\noptically\nweighted,\nnear-surface\npigment concentration\nfor bio-optical\nalgorithm\ndevelopment\n(Gordon and Clark 1980).\nWhen possible, samples should be acquired at several depths distributed throughout the upper 200 m of the water\ncolumn [or in turbid water, up to seven diffuse attenuation depths, i.e. In(E(z,_.)/E(z,_.))=7, to provide a basis for\nrelating fluorescence signals to pigment mass concentrations.\nSamples should be filtered as soon\nthan an hour, hold the samples on ice,\ndelays longer than several hours, the\nbottles, because even brief exposure to\n\nas possible after collection.\nIf processing must be delayed for more\nor in a freezer at 4\xc2\xb0C, and protect them from exposure to light. For\nsamples should be stored in liquid nitrogen.\nUse opaque sample\nlight during sampling and/or storage might alter pigment values.\n\nFiltration\nWhatman GF/F glass fiber filters, with approximately\n0.7 grn pore size, are preferred for removing\nphytoplankton\nfrom water.\nThe glass fibers assist in breaking the cells during grinding, accommodate\nlarger sample volumes, and do not form precipitate\nforms after acidification.\nTwenty-five\nmm diameter\nGF/F glass fiber filters should be used with vacuum (7-8 inches of mercury) or positive pressure (1-2 psi).\nPositive pressure filtration is recommended,\nbecause it filters larger volumes of water at reduced filtration\ntimes.\nThe only problem\nwith vacuum filtration is that unobservable\nair leaks may occur around the\nfiltration holder, and as a result the pressure gradient across the filter is much less than what is indicated on\nthe vacuum gauge.\nWhen\nobservable dripping water.\n\npositive\n\nfiltration\n\nis used,\n\n259\n\nany\n\nleakage\n\naround\n\nthe\n\nfilter\n\nholder\n\nresults\n\nin\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nInert membrane\nfilters, such as polyester filters, may be used when size fraction filtration is required.\nWhen this is done, it is recommended\nto also filter a replicate sample through a GF/F to determir, e the total\nconcentration.\nSumming the various size-fractionated\nconcentrations\nmay not produce an accurate estimate\nof the total, because of the potential for cell disruption during filtration.\nThere has been an ongoing discussion of filter types and retention efficiencies\nfor natural samples.\nPhinney\nand Yentsch\n(1985) showed the inadequacy\nof GF/F filters for retaining\nchlorophyll\na in\noligotrophic\nwaters, as did Dickson and Wheeler (1993) for samples from the North Pacific. In response to\nDickson and Wheeler (1993), Chavez et al. (1995) compared samples collected in the Pacific Ocean using\nGF/F and 0.2 Ima membrane filters with small filtered volumes (100 mL to 540 mL). Their results showed\na very close agreement between the two filter types, with GF/F filters having only a slightly positive 5 %\nbias.\nFiltration volume can directly affect the retention efficiency for GF/F filters. Particles can be retained\nby filters through a variety of ways, such as filter sieving, filter adsorption, electrostatic\nand van der Waals\nattractions\n(Brock,\n1983).\nWhen water flows through the pores of a Nuclepore\nfilter, streamlines\nare\nformed that can align small particles longitudinally,\nwith the result that cell diameter becomes important\nwith these filters.\nIt is known, on the other hand, that Whatman GF/F filters can retain particles much\nsmaller than their rated pore size. Generally, at small volumes (100 mL to 300 mL) filter adsorption,\nand\nelectrostatic\nand van der Waals attractions are important, whereas at larger volumes (>2,000 mL) sieving\ndominates.\nThis has been tested in oligotrophic\nwaters off Hawaii in which small (<500 mL) and large\nvolumes (> 2 L to 4 L) retained similar amounts of chlorophyll\na on the two types of filters, whereas for\nintermediate\nsample volumes the GF/F filters showed lower concentrations.\nDuring several cruises off the\nHawaiian\nIslands, differences\nin retention efficiencies\nwere found for GF/F filters to be a function\nof\nsample volume; large sample volumes (2 L and 4 L) retained about 18 % more chlorophyll\na than replicate\n1 L samples.\nFiltration\nvolumes are usually limited by the concentration\nof\nI-IPLC analysis it is important to filter as large a volume as possible,\nmajor pigments.\nA qualitative\ncheck to determine whether a large\ncount the number of accessory pigments (chlorophylls\nb, c_, c2, c3,\n\nparticles present in each sample.\nFor\nso as to accurately measure most of the\nenough volume has been filtered is to\nand carotenoids)\nquantified,\nexcluding\n\nchlorophyll\ndegradation\nproducts\n(Trees et al. 2000).\nMost algal groups (excluding\nphycobiliproteincontaining\ngroups) contain at least four HPLC-measurable\naccessory pigments (see Jeffrey et al. 1997).\nTherefore,\npigment samples that do not meet this minimum accessory pigment criterion may have detection\nlimit problems related to low signal-to-noise\nratios for the I-IPLC detectors and/or inadequate concentration\ntechniques\n(e.g. low filtration\nvolumes).\nIt is generally recommended\nthat the following\nvolumes be\nfiltered for HPLC pigment analyses: 3 L to 4 L for oligotrophic\nwaters, 1 L to 2 L for mesotrophic\nwaters,\nand 0.5 L to 1 L for eutrophic waters.\nIt is recommended\nto not pre-filter seawater\nsamples to remove large zooplankton\nand particles,\nbecause this practice may exclude pigment-containing\ncolonial and chain-forming\nphytoplankton,\nsuch as\ndiatoms and Trichodesmium\nsp. Forceps may be used to remove large zooplankton\nfrom the GF/Fs\nfollowing filtration.\n\nSample\n\nHandling\n\nand Storage\n\nSamples should be filtered as quickly as possible after collection and stored immediately\nin liquid\nnitrogen.\nLiquid nitrogen is the best method for storing samples with minimum degradation\nfor short, as\nwell as, longer storage times (e.g. 1 year).\nPlacing samples in liquid nitrogen also assists in pigment\nextraction\nby weakening\nthe cell wall and membrane\nduring this rapid temperature\nchange.\nUltra-cold\nfreezers (-90 \xc2\xb0C) can be used for storage, although\nthey have not been tested for longer than 60 days\n(Jeffrey et al. 1997).\nConventional\ndeep freezers should not be used for storing samples more than 20\nhours before transferring\nthem to an ultra-cold freezer, or liquid nitrogen.\nAgain, storage of samples in\nliquid nitrogen immediately\nafter filtration is the preferred method.\nSamples should be folded in half with the filtered halves facing in. This\nrubbing particles off the filter during placement in sample containers and storage.\n\n260\n\neliminates\n\nproblems\n\nof\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nIt is strongly recommended\nto use aluminum foil wrappings for sample containers.\nThis simple, but\neffective, container is both inexpensive and easy to use. Cut small pieces of heavy-duty\naluminum foil into\napproximately\n4 cm squares.\nFold each piece in half, and using a fine-point permanent\nmarker, write a\nshort sample identifier (e.g. first letter of the cruise and a sequential sample number) on the foil. Writing\non the folded foil, prior to placement of the filter, both avoids puncturing the foil with the marking pen, and\nimproves the legibility of the sample identifier.\nPlace the folded filter in the aluminum foil. Fold the three\nopen sides to form an envelope that is only slightly larger than the folded filter (-3 cmx 1.5 cm).\nThe use of foil containers\n\nminimizes\n\nthe size requirement\n\nof the storage container.\n\nIt is also acceptable\n\nto use either cryogenic\ntubes, or HistoPrep\ntissue capsules, but they occupy more storage volume per\nsample, and they are more expensive than aluminum foil. If fluorometric\nanalysis is to be done soon after\ncollection,\nit is still recommended\nto place the samples in liquid nitrogen to assist in pigment extraction,\nand on removal from the liquid nitrogen to place them immediately\nin chilled 90 % acetone.\n\nRecordkeeping\nInformation\nregarding\nsample identification\nshould be logged in a laboratory\nnotebook\nwith the\nanalyst\'s initials.\nFor each filter sample record the sample identifier (as written on the sample container),\nstation number for the cruise, water volume filtered (VFa.r) in mL, and depth of the water sample, together\nwith the date, time, latitude, and longitude of the bottle cast during which the sample was acquired.\n\n16.3\n\nLABORATORY\n\nPIGMENT\nInternal\n\nMETHODS\n\nFOR\n\nHPLC\n\nPHYTOPLANKTON\n\nANALYSIS\n\nStandard\n\nand Solvent\n\nPreparation\n\nIn addition to daily calibration of the HPLC system with external standards, an internal standard (e.g.\ncanthaxanthin)\nshould be used to determine the extraction volume. A temperature-controlled\nautosampler is optional,\nbut highly recommended,\nto chill the samples chilled prior to injection and to reduce uncertainties during\nsample preparation and injection.\nA variety of companies\nmanufacture HPLC systems (e.g. Agilent\nTechnologies,\nBeckman, ThermoQuest,\nWaters Associates).\nFor a review of hardware and software\nrequirements for measuring chlorophylls and their degradation products, as well as carotenoids, see Jeffrey\net al. (1997).\n\nHPLC Eluants\n\nand Gradient\n\nPrograms\n\nThere are several\ncurrently\nrecognized\nHPLC methods\nfor separating\nchlorophylls,\nchlorophyll\nderivatives\nand taxonomically\nimportant\ncarotenoids.\nThe Cls method\nof Wright\net al. (199I)\nis\nrecommended\nby SCOR and separates more than 50 chlorophylls,\ncarotenoids,\nand their derivatives using a\nternary gradient system. This HPLC method is described in detail below. Briefly, pigments are separated\non an Spherisorb ODS-2 Cls column using a three solvent gradient system [Solvent A: 80:20 methanol: 0.5\nM ammonium\nacetate (by volume); Solvent B: 90:10 acetonitrile:\nwater (by volume);\nSolvent C: ethyl\nacetate] at a flow rate of 1 mL min "_. The separation of the various pigments requires about 30 minutes.\nPrior to injection,\n1000 IlL of the aqueous acetone pigment extract is diluted with 300 IxL HPLC-grade\nwater to increase the affinity of pigments for the column during the loading step. This procedure\nresults in\nsharper peaks, allowing greater loading than can be obtained with undiluted samples.\nThis method does not separate monovinyl and divinyl chlorophylls\na and b.\nchlorophylls\na and b, can cause errors if they are not separated either physically\nchannels ratio method from the monovinyl\nforms.\nLatasa et al. (1996) showed\n\nThe presence of divinyl\non the column, or by a\nthat the use of a single\n\nresponse factor (only for monovinyl\nchlorophyll\na) could result in a 15 % to 25 % overestimation\nof total\nchlorophyll\na concentration if divinyl chlorophyll\na was present in significant\nconcentrations.\nAlthough\nmonovinyl and divinyl chlorophyll\na co-elute, each compound\nabsorbs differently\nat 436 nm and 450 nm\nand it is therefore possible to deconvolve the absorption signals due to these pigments (Latasa et al. 1996).\nAlternatively,\nthese two chlorophyll\nspecies can be separated chromatographically\nand individually\nquantified using the Cg HPLC techniques described by Goericke and Repeta (1993) and Van Heukelem and\nThomas (2001).\nThe latter technique uses a two solvent system and elevated column temperature\nto\nachieve desired separations.\nRegardless\nof the method or column-packing\nmaterial used (C_s or Cs), it is important that HPLC\nperformance\nbe validated before and during use. This would include validation\nthat resolution\nbetween\npeaks is acceptable,\nor when peaks are not chromatographically\nresolved, that equations based on spectral\ndeconvolution\nare possible in order to quantify relative proportions\nof each pigment in a co-eluting\npair\n(Sect. 16.4 below).\n\nDetermination\na. The use of an appropriate internal standard, such as canthaxanthin,\nwill decrease the uncertainty.\n\n16.4 QUALITY\n\nASSURANCE\n\nQuality assurance procedures\nand representative results.\n\nPROCEDURES\n\noutlined here should be routinely employed\n\nto insure accurate,\n\nprecise\n\nAs a means of monitoring an instrument\'s performance, individual pigment response factors (F _) should\nbe chatted as functions of time (Clesceri et al. 1998). These quality control graphs should be retained with\nthe data analysis logbooks\n\nto document\n\nthe quality of each data set.\n\nA selected\nnumber of samples\nshould\nbe analyzed\nin duplicate\n(or triplicate)\nto assess\nrepresentativeness\nand uncertainty in the method and instrumentation.\nIn multi-ship/investigator\nstudies,\nreplicate samples should be collected and archived for future intercalibration checks.\nFortified\n\nsamples\n\nshould\n\nbe analyzed\n\nas part of the quality assurance\n\neffort.\n\nFortified\n\nsamples\n\nare\n\nprepared in duplicate by spiking a sample with known quantities\nof the analytes\nof interest at\nconcentrations within the range expected in the samples. The\nsystem blank may not contain any of the analytes of interest above the MDL or corrective action must be\ntaken.\nA spiked blank is defined as a system blank plus an authentic external standard containing\nthe\nanalytes of interest.\nEach set of samples should be accompanied\nby a spiked blank and is quantified under\nthe same instrumental conditions as the samples.\n\n16.5\nPROTOCOL\nRESEARCH\n\nSTATUS\n\nAND\n\nFUTURE\n\nDIRECTIONS\n\nFOR\n\nRecent studies have identified the presence of novel bacterial phototrophs\nin coastal and oceanic\nwaters. These include proteorhodopsin-containing\nBacteria (B6jh et aI. 2000, 2001) and anoxygenic\naerobic phototrophic Bacteria (Kolber et al. 2000, 2001).\nSequence analysis of BAC clone libraries\nprepared from Monterey\nBay, Station\nALOHA and the Southern Ocean revealed\nthat numerous\nuncultivated\nmembers\nof the v-Proteobacteria\ncontain genes that code for proteorhodopsin.\nThis\nmembrane-bound\npigment contains trans-retinal, absorbs at blue-green to green wavelengths,\nand functions\nas a light-driven proton pump. In an unrelated study, Kolber et al. (2000) used an infrared fast repetition\nrate (IRFRR) fluorometer to document the widespread occurrence of anoxygenic\naerobic phototrophs\n(AAPS) in the world oceans. These microbes possess low amounts of bacteriochlorophyll\na (_\n= 358,\n581 and 771 nm) and unusually high levels of bacteriocarotenoids\n(gm_ = 454, 465,482\nand 514 rim). They\nrequire molecular oxygen for growth. One of us (RRB) has initiated HPLC pigment analysis of these latter\nclones and retinal-related compounds to determine if the Wright et al. (1991) method can be used for their\nseparation\n\nand quantification.\n\n265\n\nOcean Protocols For\nOptics\n\nSatellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nREFERENCES\nAbramowitz,\nPrinting),\n\nA. and I.A. Trees l, Robert\n\nR. Bidigare\n\n2, David\n\nM. Karl 2 Laurie\n\nVan Heukelem\n\n3\n\nand John Dore 2\nI Center for Hydro-Optics\n2 Department\nSHorn\n\nPoint\n\nLaboratory,\n\n& Remote\n\nSensing,\n\nof Oceanography,\nUniversity\nHorn\n\nSan Diego\nUniversity\n\nof Maryland\nPoint,\n\nCenter\n\nState\n\nUniversity,\n\nof Hawaii,\nfor\n\nCalifornia\n\nHawaii\n\nEnvironmental\n\nScience,\n\nMaryland\n\n17.1 INTRODUCTION\nIn addition to HPLC analyses, it is recommended that the standard fluorometric methodology\nused for\nmeasuring chlorophylls\nand pheopigments\nalso be applied to (i) the same extracted pigment samples used\nfor HPLC analysis,\nand (ii) additional\nindependent\nsamples.\nAnalysis of fluorometric\nchlorophyll\na\nconcentration\nis a far simpler procedure than HPLC analysis, especially at sea. On a given research cruise,\ntherefore, it is economically\nfeasible to acquire and process many more fluorometric\nthan HPLC samples\nand to statistically\nrelate fluorometric\nand HPLC chlorophyll\na concentrations\nusing linear regression\nanalysis.\nThis additional analysis will also enable a direct link to the historical bio-optical\nalgorithms and\ndatabase development\nduring the CZCS validation experiments.\nProtocols\n\nfor fluorometric\n\ndetermination\n\nof the concentrations\n\nof chlorophyll\n\nand pheopigments\n\nwere\n\ndeveloped\ninitially by Yentsch and Menzel (1963) and Holm-Hansen\net al. (1965), and are described\nin\ndetail by Strickland and Parsons (1972). Holm-Hansen\net al. (1965) and Stfickland andParsons\n(1972) used\nfirst principles of fluorescence\nspectroscopy\nto derive these fluorometric\nequations. The equation proposed\nby Yentsch and Menzel (i963) is only indirectly linked to first principles, through debatable assumptions,\nand its use is not recommended.\nAlthough\nthese measurements\nhave been shown to contain errors as\ncompared\nto HPLC determinations\n(Trees et al. 1985; Smith et al. 1987; Hoepffner\nand Sathyendranath\n1992; Bianchi et al. 1995; Tester et al. 1995), the CZCS phytoplankton\npigment concentration\nalgorithms\nwere based on them entirely. The SeaWiFS protocols for this analysis will be those given in Strickland and\nParsons (1972) as updated by this chapter.\nPigment databases generally show a log-normal distribution,\nwhich is consistent with that proposed by\nCampbell (1995) for bio-optical properties.\nTherefore, it is appropriate to perform log-linear regressions on\nHPLC determined\ntotal chlorophyll\na (chlorophyllide\na, chlorophyll\na epimer, chlorophyll\na allomer,\nmonovinyl\nchlorophyll\na and divinyl chlorophyll\na) and fluorometrically\ndetermined\nchlorophyll\na, using\nmodel I regressions.\nStandard\nModel\nI regressions\nwere selected\nbecause\nHPLC determined\ntotal\nchlorophyll\na concentrations\nare to be predicted from fluorometrically\ndetermined\nchlorophyll\n[Model I\nregressions\nare appropriate\nfor both predictions\nand determining\nfunctional relationships,\nwhereas Model II\nregressions\nshould not be used to predict values of y given x (page 543, Sokal and Rohlf 1995)].\nExamples of regression\nmodels predicting log HPLC total chlorophyll\na (following Chapter 16 HPLC\nprotocols) from log fluorometric\nchlorophyll\na are shown in Figures 17.1, 17.2, and 17.3 for three cruises\nin different geographic areas. In each example, the regression slopes are significantly\ndifferent from a oneto-one relationship,\nalthough for the Gulf of California (GoCAL November\n1996, Figure 17.3) the slope is\nclose to unity.\nOne-to-one\nratios have also been found for other geographic\nareas, but not necessarily\nduring all seasons.\nTherefore,\nthe relationship\n(slope and offset) between HPLC total chlorophyll\na and\nfluorometric\nchlorophyll\na must be determined\nfor a selected number of samples for each cruise, so that a\ncruise-specific\nscaling factor can be applied to other fluorometric\nsamples.\n\n269\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nThe protocols specified below for fluorometric\nchlorophyll\na analyses follow closely those prescribed\nin the JGOFS Core Measurement\nProtocols (UNESCO\n1994), but they differ in one importvaat respect.\nAbsorption\nof light in seawater, or any other medium, is a volumetric\nprocess, even though :he volume\nabsorption\ncoefficient\nmay vary with the density of the medium.\nFor ocean color and optic\'d analyses,\ntherefore,\nthe concentration\nof chlorophyll\na shall be expressed\nin units of mass per unit volume\nof\nseawater,\neither in _tg L -1, or mg m 3.\nThis differs from the IGOFS protocols,\nwhich specify that\nconcentrations\nin seawater of chlorophyll\na and pheopigments\nshould be expressed in _tg kg _.\n\n17.2 SAMPLE\n\nACQUISITION\n\nAND STORAGE\n\nWater samples should be taken using, e.g., Niskin bottles at the site of, and simultaneously\nwith, the\nsurface in-water upwelled radiance and reflectance\nmeasurements,\nand at depth increments\nsufficient to\nresolve variability within at least the top optical depth.\nThe K(z), profiles over this layer will be used to compute optically weighted, near-surface\npigment\nconcentration\nfor bio-optical\nalgorithm development\n(Gordon and Clark 1980). When possible, samples\nshould also be acquired at several depths distributed throughout the upper 200 m of the water column [or in\nturbid water, up to seven diffuse attenuation depths, i.e. ln(E(0)/E(z))=7,\nto provide a basis for relating\nfluorescence\nsignals to pigment mass concentration.\nSamples should be filtered as soon\nthan an hour, hold the samples on ice,\ndelays longer than several hours, the\nbottles, because even brief exposure to\n\nas possible after collection.\nIf processing must be delayed for more\nor in a freezer at 4\xc2\xb0C, and protect them from exposure to light. For\nsamples should be stored in liquid nitrogen.\nUse opaque sample\nlight during sampling and/or storage might alter pigment values.\n\nFiltration\nhatman GF/F glass fiber filters, with approximately\n0.7 _tm pore size, are preferred\nfor removing\nphytoplankton\nfrom water. The glass fibers assist in breaking the cells during grinding and no precipitate\nforms after acidification.\nTwenty-five\nmm diameter GF/F glass fiber filters should be used with a vacuum\nor positive pressure with a pressure differential\nequivalent\nvolumes are not required, because of the increased sensitivity\n\nto 180-200 mm of mercury.\nLarge\nof the fluorescence\nmeasurement.\n\nfiltration\n\nInert membrane filters, such as polyester filters, may be used when size fraction filtration is required.\nWhen this is done, it is recommended\nto also filter a replicate sample through a GF/F to determine the total\nconcentration.\nSumming the various size-fractionated\nconcentrations\nmay not produce an accurate estimate\nof the total, because of the potential for cell disruption during filtration.\nThere has been an ongoing discussion on filter types and retention efficiencies\nfor natural samples.\nPhinney\n& Yentsch (1985) showed\nthe inadequacy\nof GF/F filters for retaining\nchlorophyll\na in\noligotrophic\nwaters, as did Dickson and Wheeler (1993) for samples from the North Pacific. In response to\nDickson and Wheeler (1993), Chavez et al. (1995) compared samples collected in the Pacific Ocean using\nGF/F and 0.2 lxm membrane filters with small filtered volumes (100-540 mL).\nTheir results for small\nvolumes showed a very close agreement\nbetween the two filter types with GF/F filters having only a\nslightly positive 5% bias.\nFiltration volume can directly affect the retention efficiency for GF/F filters. Particles can be retained\nby filters through a variety of ways, such as filter sieving, filter adsorption,\nelectrostatic\nand van der Waals\nattractions\n(Brock, 1983).\nWhen water flows through the pores of a Nuclepore\nfilter, streamlines\nare\nformed that can align small partj\'cles longitudinally,\nwith the result that cell diameter becomes important\nwith these filters.\nIt is known, on the other hand, that Whatman GF/F filters can retain particles much\nsmaller than their rated pore size.\nGenerally,\nat small volumes (100-300\nmL) filter adsorption,\nand\nelectrostatic\nand van der Waals attractions are important, whereas at larger volumes (> 2,000 mL) sieving\ndominates.\nThis has been tested in oligotrophic\nwaters off Hawaii in which small (< 500 mL) and large\nvolumes (> 2-4 liters) retained similar amounts of chlorophyll\na on the two types of filters, whereas for\nintermediate\nsample volumes the GF/F filters showed lower concentrations.\nAs a general rule, it is\nrecommended\nthat the following volumes be filtered for these water types: 0.5-1.0 liter for oligotrophic,\n0.2-0.5 liter for mesotrophic,\nand 0.1 liter and less for eutrophic water.\n\n270\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite\nOptics\nFor\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nIt is recommended\nto not pre-filter\nseawater\nsamples to remove large zooplankton\nand particles,\nbecause this practice may exclude pigment-containing\ncolonial and chain-forming\nphytoplankton,\nsuch as\ndiatoms and Trichodesmium\nsp. Forceps should be used to remove large zooplankton\nfrom the GF/Fs\nfollowing filtration.\n\nSample\n\nHandling,\n\nand Storage\n\nSamples should be filtered as quickly as possible after collection, and the filters stored immediately\nin\nliquid nitrogen.\nLiquid nitrogen is the best method for storing filter samples with minimum degradation\nfor\nshort, as well as, longer storage times (e.g. 1 year).\nPlacing samples in liquid nitrogen also assists in\npigment extraction\nby weakening the cell wall and membrane during this rapid temperature\nchange.\nUltracold freezers (-90\xc2\xb0C) can be used for storage, although they have not been tested for longer than 60 days\n(Jeffrey et al. 1997).\nConventional\ndeep freezers should not be used for storing samples more than 20\nhours before transferring\nthem to an ultra-cold freezer, or liquid nitrogen.\nAgain, storage of samples in liquid nitrogen immediately\nafter filtration is the preferred method.\nThe\naddition of MgCO3 at the end of the filtration process to stabilize chlorophyll\nhas not been used for many\nyears as a routine\n\noceanographic\n\nmethod,\n\nbecause\n\nof the uncertainty\n\nin pigment\n\nabsorption\n\nby MgCO3.\n\nIf samples are to be stored for any length of time prior to fluorometric\nanalysis, they should be folded\nin half with the filtered halves facing in. This eliminates problems of rubbing particles off the filter during\nplacement\n\nin sample\n\ncontainers\n\nand storage.\n\nIt is strongly recommended\nto use aluminum foil wrappings for sample containers.\nThis simple, but\neffective, container is both inexpensive and easy to use. Cut small pieces of heavy-duty\naluminum foil into\napproximately\n4 cm squares.\nFold each piece in half, and using a fine-point permanent\nmarker, write a\nshort sample identifier (e.g. fast letter of the cruise and a sequential sample number) on the foil. Writing\non the folded foil, prior to placement of the filter, both avoids puncturing the foil with the marking pen, and\nimproves the legibility of the sample identifier.\nPlace the folded filter in the aluminum foil. Fold the three\nopen sides to form an envelope that is only slightly larger than the folded filter (~3cm x 1.5era).\nThe use of foil containers minimizes the size requirement\nof the storage container.\nIt is also acceptable\nto use either cryogenic\ntubes, or HistoPrep\ntissue capsules,\nbut they occupy more storage volume per\nsample, and they are more expensive than aluminum foil. If fluorometric\nanalysis is to be done soon after\ncollection,\nit is still recommended\nto place the samples in liquid nitrogen to assist in pigment extraction,\nand on removal from the liquid nitrogen toplace them immediately\nin chilled 90% acetone.\n\nRecordkeeping\nInformation\nregarding\nsample identification\nshould be logged in a laboratory\nnotebook\nwith the\nanalyst\'s initials.\nFor each filter sample record the sample identifier (as written on the sample container),\nstation number for the cruise, water volume filtered (VnLT) in mL, and depth of the water sample, together\nwith the date, time, latitude, and longitude of the bottle cast during which the sample was acquired.\n\n17.3 LABORATORY\nDETERMINATION\nCONCENTRATIONS\n\nMETHODS\nFOR FLUOROMETRIC\nOF CHL. a AND PHEOPIGMENT\n\nChlorophyll\nand pheopigments\ncan be determined using either a Turner Designs\n(or Sequoia)\nfluorometers\nequipped\nwith the standard light sources and Coming excitation\nand emission\nfilters,\nfollowing\nthe manufacture\'s\nrecommendation\nfor measuring\nextracted\nchlorophyll.\nThe fluorometric\ninstrument should be warmed-up for at least 30 to 45 minutes prior to making measurements.\nBecause of the acidification requirement for the standard fluorometric method (Holm-Hansen\net al.\n1965), differences\nin excitation\nand emission\nwavelength\nbands between\nfluorometers\ncan produce\nuncertainties (Trees et al. 1985). The sensitivity with which a particular instrument is able to differentiate\nbetween chlorophyll and pheopigment\nis a function of the excitation wavelength. This effect is measured\n\n271\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nduring calibration\nof the fluorometer\nand is called the tau factor (\'0. Saijo and Nishizawa\n(1969) have\nshown that _ can vary from I to 11.5, depending upon the excitation wavelength\n(in the range between\n410 nm and 440 nm). For example, a comparison\nbetween\naTurner Designs (Model 10-005R) analog\nfluorometer\nand a Turner Designs (Model 10-AU-005)\ndigital fluorometer\nshowed statistically\nsignificant\ndifferences\nfor 42 oceanic samples (slope = 1.06), even though both were calibrated with exactly the same\nstandards\n(Figure 17.4).\nThe departure from a unit slope is attributable\nto differences\nin the excitation\nbands for the two fluorometers.\n\nFluorometer\n\nCalibrations\n\nBench fluorometers\nused to measure concentrations\nof extracted\nbe calibrated using authentic chlorophyll\na standards, as prescribed\n16). Chlorophyll\na standards can be purchased from Sigma Chemical\n\nchlorophyll\nand pheopigments\nshould\nalso in the HPLC Protocols (Chapter\nCo. (St. Louis, MO 63178, USA).\n\nIf a fluorometer\nhas been shipped for a cruise, or if it has been unused for several weeks, it is strongly\nrecommended\nthat it be recalibrated\nwith an authentic chlorophyll\na standard.\nThe use of solid standards,\nlike those provided by Turner Designs and other manufacturers,\ncan only provide a check for instrumental\ndrift. They cannot be used as primary pigment standards.\nHowever, the solid standard should be used at\nfrequent intervals during each day\'s analyses to monitor instrument drift.\nThe concentration\nof the chlorophyll\na standard, in the appropriate solvent, must be determined\nusing a\nmonochromator-based\nspectrophotometer\nprior to calibrating\nthe fluorometer.\nThe recommended\nextinction coefficients\nfor chlorophyll\na in several solvents can be found in Appendix E of Jeffrey et al.\n(1997). Absorbance\nis measured in a 1 cm cuvette at the peak wavelength\n_,\nand at 750 nm to correct for\nlight scattering.\nThe bandwidth\nof the spectrophotometer\nshould be between 0.5 and 2 gra, with the\nstandard concentration\nbeing such that the absorbance\nfalls between 0.1 and 1.0 optical density units\n(Clesceri et al., 1998a). The concentration\nof the standard is calculated as\n106[A(_,_)-\n\nA(750)]\n\nCsrv =\n\n,\n\n(17.1)\n\nb/_m\nwhere\n\nCs.m is the concentration\n\nabsorbances\ncoefficient\n\nat _,_\n\nand 750 rim, b is the pathlength\n\n(L g-i cm-]) of chlorophyll\n\n100% acetone\n_,_\n\n(I.tg L "t) of the chlorophyll\n\n(3effrey et al. 1997, Appendix\n\nof cuvette\n\na in 90% acetone.\n\nEl=, =88.15 L g-! cm-1, when applied\n\na standard,\n\nA(_,_,_)\n\n(cm), and Elm\n\nFor 90% acetone\n\nto the absorption\n\nE). Standards stored under nitrogen in the dark at -20\xc2\xb0C do not\nchange appreciably\nover a one-month period, provided that they are stored in containers proven to prevent\nevaporation\n(e.g. glass or Teflon bottles/vials).\n\nThe stock chlorophyll\na standard, with its concentration\nmeasured on a spectrophotometer\nas described\nabove, should be diluted using calibrated gas-tight syringes, and Class A volumetric\npipettes and flasks.\nThe minimum number of dilutions of the stock standard for calibrating a fluorometer depends on whether it\nis a digital model (Turner Designs 10-AU-005),\nor it is an analog model with a mechanical\nmode for\nchanging sensitivity\n(e.g. Turner Designs 10-005). A minimum of 5 dilutions is required for calibrating a\ndigital fluorometer.\nAnalog fluorometers\nwith a variety of door settings, such as the Turner Designs Model\n10-005, must be calibrated for each door setting using at least three standard concentrations\nper door. The\ndiluted standard pigment concentrations\nused in calibrating\nthe fluorometer\nmust bracket the range of\nconcentrations\nfound in the samples being analyzed.\nEach diluted chlorophyll\na standard is placed in the fluorometer\nand the signal (Fb) is recorded, after\nwaiting a short period of time (60 seconds) for it to stabilize.\nThe standard is removed and diluted HCL\nacid (2 drops of 5 %, or 1 drop of 10 %, both concentrations\nby volume) is added and mixed within the test\ntube. The tube is then placed back into the fluorometer,\nand after stabilization,\nthe acidified fluorescence\n\n272\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite Ocean\nOptics\nFor\n\nColor Sensor Validation\n\nsignal (F,) is recorded.\nFollowing acidification\nof the chlorophyll\na standard, the fluorescence\nsignal\nstabilizes\nrelatively quickly.\nThis is not the case for natural samples that contain a mixture of pigment\ncompounds,\nhowever, and stabilization\ntime may vary from sample to sample. Stabilization\ntime has to be\nthe same for both pigment standards and for natural samples. To minimize this source of uncertainty,\nand to\nstandardize this measurement\ntechnique, it is recommended\nthat both acidified natural sample and acidified\npigment\nstandards\nbe allowed to react with the acid for one minute prior to recording\nthe acidified\nfluorescence\nsignal (Fa). Two drops of 5 % Coy volume) hydrochloric\nacid is added to each of the pigment\nstandards and natural samples.\nOnce the acid is added, the sample in the test tube should be mixed by\ninverting\nthe tube several times, using parafilm as a stopper.\nAll fluorometric\nmeasurements\nfor both\npigment standards and natural samples should be carded out at room temperature.\nA 90 % Coy volume)\nacetone blank (Blkb) and an acidified\nacetone blank (B/k_) should also be measured,\neven though the\nacidified blank (Blka) is frequently\nfound to be equal to the non-acidified\nblank (BIkb). The fluorometer\'s\nsensitivity\n\nto pheopigments,\n\nx, is calculated\n\nas\n\nFb- Bl ,\nT = F_ - Blk\'-----_,"\n\n(17.2)\n\nand is averaged over all concentrations\nof the chlorophyll a standard. For the mechanical door model\nfluorometers,\ndata from the higher gain door settings will often become noisy and computed x values will\nbegin to decrease.\nThese data should be excluded from the average. The fluorometer\'s\nresponse factor, FR\n0.tg L "1per fluorescence\nsignal), is determined\nas the slope of the simple linear regression equation\nC_\n\n-- F, (f b - Bike),\n\n(17.3)\n\ncalculated for the sample of diluted concentrations\nof the pigment standard, and forcing a zero intercept.\nWith a digital fluorometer,\nthe regression analysis is applied to the data from the entire 5, or more,\nconcentrations\nand a single FR factor is determined\nfor the instrument.\nWith a mechanical fluorometer,\nthe\nregression is applied to the data from the 3, or more, concentrations\nof the standard, and a separate FR factor\nis determined,\nfor each door setting. As a means of monitoring an instrument\'s performance,\nFR factors\nfrom successive calibrations\nshould be charted as functions of time (Clesceri et al., 1998b). These quality\ncontrol graphs should be retained with the data analysis logbooks to document the quality of each data set\nfor which that fluorometer is used.\n\nSolvent\n\nPreparation.\n\nIt is recommended\nthat 90 % acetone Coy volume) be used to extract pigments for the fluorometric\nanalysis.\nRichard and Thompson\n(1952) were the In\'st to propose 90 % acetone as a solvent to extract\npigments from marine phytoplankton.\nTheir results indicated improved extraction efficiencies,\nand also\nshowed that the procedure\nminimized the activity of the naturally occurring chlorophyllase\nenzyme, which\ndegrades the pigment.\nWith a graduated cylinder, make up 90 % acetone by first pouring in distilled water,\nfollowed\nby 100 % acetone.\nUsing volumetric\npipettes, or auto-pipettes,\naccurately\nmeasure 8 ml_. to\n10 mL of 90 % acetone and place it in a centrifuge tube. Record this volume as VEx-r. A number of such\ntubes containing\nacetone are then stored in a freezer and individually\nremoved as filter samples\nare\ncollected.\nPre-chilling\nthe solvent in this way reduces the possibility\nof temperature\ninduced pigment\ndegradation.\n\nExtraction\nFilters are removed\nfrom liquid nitrogen and placed in the chilled centrifuge tubes for extraction\nin\nVEx-r mL of 90% acetone.\nSamples are disrupted by sonication, placed in a freezer, and allowed to extract\nat 0\xc2\xb0(2 for 24 h. Alternatively,\nthe cells can be mechanically\ndisrupted using a glass/Teflon\ntissue grinder\nand allowed to extract at 0\xc2\xb0C for 24 h. If after disrupting the cells, it is necessary to rinse the tissue grinder,\nor mortar and pestle, then a known volume of 90% acetone, measured using a Class A volumetric\npipette,\nshould be used.\nThe ease at which the pigments are removed from the cells varies considerably\nwith\ndifferent phytoplankton.\nIn all cases, freezing the sample filters in liquid nitrogen improves extraction\nefficiency.\nPrior to analysis, pigment extracts are swirled into a vortex to remove particles from the sides\nof the tube, and then centrifuged to minimize cellular debris.\n\n273\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite Color\nOptics\nFor\nOcean Sensor\nValidation\nMeasurement\nFollowing\nthe same measurement\nprocedure\ndescribed\nabove under Fluorometer\nCalibretion,\neach\nextracted sample is placed in the fluorometer\nand its non-acidified\nand acidified responses, Fb a,ld Fa, are\nmeasured and recorded. The concentration\nof chlorophyll\n[Chl] (_tg L -1) in the sample is calculat,..d as\nV_.x-r\n[Chl] = (F b- F_ - Blk b + Blk, )-_\nand pheopigments\n\nconcentration\n\n--\n\nFR\n\nVFILT\n\n(17.4)\n\n_\n\n[Pheo] (I.tg L -1) as\n\n[ Pheo] = {( F - Blk, )c-(\n\nFb - Blkb )}-_-_\n\nFR V_cr\n\nVFIL T \'\n\n(17.5)\n\nwhere volumes extracted VEx\'r and filtered Vmr are in mL. Pheopigment\nconcentrations\ndetermined\nusing\nthe standard fluorometric\nmethod of Holm-Hansen\net al. (1965) have not been reported in published articles\nfor many years. This is based on the fact that (i) there is always a residual amount of pheopigments\nin all\nnatural samples\n(Smith and Baker, 1978; 25% of the summed chlorophyll\nplus pheopigment),\n(ii)\npheopigment\nconcentrations\nare overestimated\nin the presence of chlorophyll\nb (Lorenzen\nand Jeffrey,\n1980; Vernet and Lorenzen,\n1987), and (iii) HPLC measured pheopigments,\ngenerally contribute very little\nto the chlorophyll\na pigment pool (e.g., Hallegraeff,\n1981; Everitt et al., 1990; and Bricaud et al., 1995).\nTrees et aL (2000a) assembled\nan extensive HPLC pigment database (5,617 samples) extending\nover a\ndecade of sampling and analysis, and including a variety of environments\nranging from freshwater\nto\nmarine, oligotrophic\nto eutrophic,\nand tropical to polar, and found that the average pheopigment\nto\nchlorophyll\na ratio was only 0.037.\nThis global scale result emphasizes\nthe problems\nassociated\nwith\nestimating pheopigments\nusing the standard fluorometric\nmethod.\n\n17.4 In Situ CHLOROPHYLL\nAn in situ fluorometer\nfluorescence.\nThe fluorometer\n\nshould\nshould\n\na FLUORESCENCE\n\nPROFILES\n\nbe employed\nto measure\na continuous\nprofile of chlorophyll\nbe mounted on the same underwater package as the water sampler,\n\nideally together with a CTD, transrnissometer\nand other inherent optical properties (IOP) sensors.\ncases it may be desirable to also include a radiometer on this package, if shading effects associated\npackage and/or ship are not significant.\n\nIn some\nwith the\n\nIn situ fluorometers\nproduce nearly continuous\nprofiles of artificially\nstimulated\nfluorescence.\nFluorometer\ndata (in volts) should be corrected by subtracting\nan offset, determined by shading the\ninstrument on deck. These unscaled fluorescence responses are adequate to provide guidance in K-profile\nanalysis and interpretation.\nTo produce\nvertical\ncontinuous\nprofiles\nof pigment\nconcentration,\nHPLC-derived\npigment\nconcentrations\nfrom water samples taken at discrete depths may be interpolated,\nwith the aid of in situ\nfluorescence profiles. These fluorescence\ninterpolated profiles should then be used with Kd(Z,_,) profiles to\ncompute the optically weighted average pigment concentration over the top attenuation length (Gordon and\nClark 1980).\nThe A/D channel used to acquire and record signal voltages from the in situ fluorometer\nmust be\ncalibrated,\nand its temperature-dependent\nresponse\nto known voltage inputs characterized.\nThe range\ndependent A/D bias coefficients\nshould be determined at approximately\n50 C intervals over the range from\n0-250 C to characterize\nthe temperature\nsensitivity of the data acquisition system.\nZero fluorescence\noffsets should be measured on deck before and after each cast; the optical windows\nshould be shaded to avoid contamination\nof the zero offset value by ambient light. Before each cast, the\nfluorometer\nwindows should be cleaned following the manufacturer\'s\ninstructions.\n\n274\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\n17.5 PROTOCOL\nRESEARCH\n\nSTATUS\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nAND FUTURE\n\nDIRECTIONS\n\nFOR\n\nIn order to minimize interferences\ncaused by the overlapping\nexcitation and emission wavebands\nof\nchlorophylls\na, b, c and pheopigments,\nTurner Designs (Sunnyvale,\nCA) manufactures\nthe multi-spectral\nfluorometer\nTD-700. Jeremy\nJ Science\n\nWerdell\nSystems\n\n2Futuretech\n3Science\n\nApplications\n\n18\n\nData Protocols\nI, Sean\nand\n\nBailey\n\nz, and\n\nApplications\n\nand Policy\nGiulietta\n\nInc.,\n\nCorporation,\n\nLanham,\n\nGreenbelt,\n\nInternational\n\nCorporation,\n\nS. Fargion\n\n3\n\nMaryland\n\nMaryland\nBeltsville,\n\nMaryland\n\n18.1 INTRODUCTION\nThe SeaWiFS Project developed the SeaWiFS Bio-optical\nArchive and Storage\nbe a local repository for in situ optical and pigment data products regularly used in\nanalyses. Information\non the original SeaBASS design is provided in Hooker et al.\nbeen expanded to contain data sets collected by participants\nof the SIMBIOS Project.\nof the SeaBASS system is available via the World Wide Web:\n\nSystem (SeaBASS) to\na variety of scientific\n(1994), and has since\nA detailed description\n\n<http://seabass.gsfc.nasa.gov>.\nBoth the SeaWiFS and SIMBIOS Projects use in situ bio-optical\ndata for the validation of SeaWiFS\nand other satellite (e.g., OCTS and POLDER) data products, and for the development\nof new ocean color\nalgorithms.\nIn addition, SeaBASS\nsupports international\nprotocol workshops,\ndata merger studies, and\ntime-series\nstudies. Archived\ndata include measurements\nof water-leaving\nradiance, chlorophyll\na, and\nother related optical and pigment parameters.\nWhen available, additional oceanographic\nand atmospheric\ndata (given in Table 2.1) are also archived in SeaBASS.\nData are collected\nby a number of different\ninstrument\npackages,\nsuch as profilers, buoys, and above-water\nmeasurement\ndevices, on a variety of\nplatforms, including ships, moorings, and drifters. The contents of SeaBASS are made readily available to\nthe SIMBIOS and MODIS Science Team Members, and to other approved individuals\n(e.g., members of\nother ocean color instrument\nteams, volunteer-contributing\nresearchers,\netc.) on a case-by-case\nbasis.\nAccess to the database and data archive is available to authorized users through the SeaBASS Web page.\nAs SIMBIOS US Science Team members are contractually\nobligated to provide data to SeaBASS, the\nvolume of archived data is rapidly increasing (McClain and Fargion 1999a and 1999b). With the launch of\nMODIS,\nas well as a number of present and upcoming\ninternational\nmissions (e.g., GLI, POLDER-2,\nMERIS, OCI, OCM, etc.), the use of the SeaBASS data archive is expected to increase dramatically\nas\nthese missions begin to require validation data.\n\n18.2 SeaBASS\n\nDATA\n\nFORMAT\n\nSeaBASS presently contains over 22,000 bio-optical\ndata files, encompassing\nmore than 650 separate\nexperiments.\nIn addition, its historical pigment database holds over 286,000 records of phytoplankton\npigment data. To account for the continuous growth of the data archive, the Project believed it essential to\ndevelop efficient data ingestion and storage techniques. Such ingestion procedures and protocols were\ndesigned to be as straightforward\nand effortless as possible on the part of the contributing investigators,\nwhile still offering a useful format for internal analysis efforts. The Project considered the following to be\nthe most important in the design of the system:\n1.\n\nSimple data format, easily read and updated;\n\n2.\n\nGlobal portability across multiple\n\n3.\n\nWeb accessible\n\ncomputer platforms;\n\ndata holdings.\n\n284\n\nand\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nAs a result, SeaBASS\nsupports standard, flat (two-dimensional)\nASCII text files, which are easily\nmanaged\nfrom any computer\nplatform\nand by most programming\nlanguages.\nThe architecture\nof a\nSeaBASS data file is simple: data are presented in columns (delimited\nby spaces, tabs, or commas)\nand\npreceded by a series of predefined metadata headers. The headers provide descriptive\ninformation\non the\ndata file, such as date, time, location, investigators,\ncolumn names and units, and additional\nancillary\ninformation.\nSeveral examples of SeaBASS data files are available both online:\n\nand in Appendix\n\nB. Appendix\n\n18.3 SeaBASS\n\n<http://seabass.gsfc.nasa.gov/seabass_submit.html>\nB provides a detailed description\nof the SeaBASS\n\nfile format.\n\nARCHITECTURE\n\nSeaBASS\ncontains two separate but linked entities, a data archive and two relational databases\n(RDBs), the Historical Pigment RDB and the Bio-Optical\nRDB. The data archive_ consists of a directory\n_\ntree where the physical data files and documentation\nfor the Bio-Optical\nRDB are stored. Main directories\nand subdirectories\nare organized by contributor,\naffiliation, experiment,\nand specific cruise. Each cruise has\nadditional subdirectories\ncontaining the in situ data sets, relevant documentation,\nand instrument calibration\nfiles associated\nwith that cruise. Authorized\nusers may peruse the directory tree via the SeaBASS\nWeb\npage.\nThe two RDBs were built using the SQL Server product from Sybase, Inc. The Historical\nPigment\nRDB consists of a single database table with over 286,000 records of phytoplankton\npigment data. These\ndata are available to the general public, but are not currently maintained or updated. Access to the holdings\nof the Historical Pigment RDB is provided via an online search engine:\n<http:llseabass.gsfc.nasa.govlcgibinlpigmentquery.cgi>.\nOver the past year, the Bio-Optical\nRDB has been expanded to include 19 database tables for storage\nof both metadata information\nand geophysical\ndata values. Half of the tables store general information\nabout each data file (e.g., provided\nin the headers of each file) and additional\ninformation\nabout\ncontributing\nresearchers\nand standard SeaBASS\nfield names and units. The others are used to store\nancillary information\nabout each data record (e.g., date, time, location, measurement\ndepth, and station) and\nthe geophysical\ndata values. Other changes to the Bio-Optical\ndatabase include:\n1.\n\nImproved\n\n2.\n\nA reconfigured\nphysical\n\n3.\n\ndata normalization;\n\nstorage\n\nThe development\nactivities.\n\nsystem\n\nwhich takes advantage\n\nof multiple\n\ncomputer\n\nfor internal\n\nSIMBIOS\n\nprocessors\n\nand increased\n\nspace; and\nof software\n\nand applications\n\nProject\n\nOffice\n\naccounting\n\nSeveral online search engines allow users to access the holdings of the Bio-Optical\nRDB, either by\npointing to files in the data archive or by returning geophysical\nvalues. Four are described below, one of\nwhich is available to the general public. The General Information\nSearch Engine\n\nis available\nparameters\n\n<http:llseabass.gsfc.nasa.govlcgi-birggeneral_info.cgi>\nto the public and allows users to retrieve a list of archived cruises and the data types and\ncollected on each. The SeaBASS Bio-Optical\nSearch Engine\n\n<http:llseabass\'gsfc\'nasa\'g\xc2\xb0vlpr\xc2\xb0tlseabass-query\'cgt>\npermits users to access all of the Bio-Optical\ndata holdings and returns a list of matching\navailable to view or download. The SeaBASS Pigment Locator:\n\nfiles, which are\n\n<http:llseabass.gsfc.nasa.govlprotlpigment_locator.c_>\nprovides direct access to the phytoplankton\nSeaBASS Aerosol Locator\n\npigment\n\ndata stored in the Bio-Optical\n\n<http:/Iseabass.gsfc.nasa.govlprotlaerosol_locator.cgi>\n\n285\n\nRDB. Likewise,\n\nthe\n\nOcean Protocols Satellite Color\nOptics\nFor\nOcean Sensor\nValidation\nprovides access\ndirect\ntoAOTdata\nstored Bio-Optical When\ninthe\nRDB.\nusing latter\nthe\ntwosearch\nengines,\nqueries\nreturn\nalistofgeophysical which\nvalues\nareavailable\ntoviewordownload. search\nEach\nenginellows\na\nusersolimitsearches\nt\ntoparticular\nexperiments, contributors,\ncruises,\norparameters\n(e.g.,\nSPM AOT)and\nand\ntoapply\nspecificate location\nd and\nranges.\nTheSIMBIOS\nProject\nOffice\nregularlyevelops and\nd\nnew improved\nWeb-based foraccessing\ntools\nand\nviewing\ntheholdingsfSeaBASS. tosuch\no\nLinks\nresources alwaysrovided\nare\np\nat\n<http\n:llseabass.gsfc.nasa.gov/dataordering.html>.\n18.4\n\nDATA\n\nQUALITY\n\nTo assist with the standardization\n\nof SeaBASS\n\ndata files, the Project\n\ndeveloped\n\nfeedback\n\nsoftware\n\nand\n\nprotocols to evaluate the format of submitted data files. The primary component of the software is known\nas FCHECK.\nFCHECK\nconsists of a Practical Extraction\nand Report Language\n(PERL)\nscript with\nconnections\nto several look-up tables and UNIX mail handling utilities.\nData contributors,\nusing any computer platform at their disposal, may test a data file for compatibility\nwith the SeaBASS format by sending the file via electronic mail to fcheck@seabass.gsfc.nasa.gov.\nUpon\nreceipt of the file, FCHECK parses the data and metadata and compares it to the required SeaBASS format.\nResults of this analysis are electronically\nmailed to the contributor and to the SeaBASS administrator.\nThis\nformat analysis requires little-to-no intervention\non behalf of the administrator\nand has proven to reduce\nconsiderably\nthe amount of processing time needed for both the administrator\nand contributor.\nIn late 2000,\na file transfer protocol (FTP) version of FHECK\nwas developed\nto assist contributors\nin evaluating\n(simultaneously)\nlarge volumes of data. Additional information on FCHECK is available online:\n<http:llseabass.gsfc.nasa.gov/fcheck_desc.html>.\nOnce data are prepared\nfor archiving, the contributor\nuploads the data and calibration\nfiles,\nsupporting\ndocumentation\nto Sea.BASS via FTP. The administrator\nthen collects the files and evaluates\ndata set. With regards to data format and content, the following requirements\nmust be met:\n1.\n\nData files must be organized\nerrors);\n\n2.\n\nSupporting\n\n3.\n\nThe documentation\n\'calibration_files\'\n\ndocumentation\n\nin the proper\n\nand calibration\n\nSea.BASS format (i.e., FCHECK\n\nfiles must be included\n\nand\nthe\n\ndoes not report any\n\nin the submission;\n\nand calibration files must match those listed in the \'documents\'\nheaders in each data file.\n\nand\nand\n\nThe documentation\nand calibration\nfiles are inspected for completeness.\nAt a minimum,\nthe Project\nrequires that documentation\ninclude a cruise report or station log (with ancillary information\nsuch as date,\ntime, location coordinates,\nwater depth, sea and sky states, observations,\nand notes) and an instrument\nreport (with information\nsuch as instruments\nused, processing methods, equations, and references).\nThe\nProject encourages\nthe contributor to include additional documentation,\nsuch as digital photographs\nof sea\nand sky states. Calibration\nfiles must include calibration coefficients\nand the date each instrument\nwas\ncalibrated.\nOnce the data set has passed visual inspection,\nthe administrator\narchives the data files and\ningests the appropriate information\ninto the database. At this point, the new data become available online to\nthe Science Team.\n\n18.5 ACCESS\n\nPOLICY\n\nAND USERS\n\nThe SeaBASS Data Access Policy applies to data submitted to the NASA SIMBIOS Project at GSFC\nfor inclusion in the calibration and validation data collection. An update to the SeaWiFS Data Policy can be\nfound in Firestone and Hooker (2001). The SIMBIOS investigators must, at a minimum, comply with\nSIMBIOS data policy, although the Project encourages a more open policy.\nOcean color algorithm development\nis severely observation limited. As such, rapid turnaround\nand\naccess to field data are essential to advance the state of the art. Data obtained under SIMBIOS NRA-99\ncontracts must be submitted no later than six months from the date of collection. International SIMBIOS\n\n286\n\nOcean Protocols\nOptics\nScience\n\nTeam\n\nMERIS,\n\netc.) are encouraged\n\nand researchers\n\ninvolved\nto provide\n\nFor Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation\n\nin other\n\nocean\n\ncolor\n\nmissions\n\n(i.e.,\n\nPOLDER,\n\nGLI,\n\nMODIS,\n\ntheir data as well, in order to foster collaboration.\n\nFor a period of three years following data collection,\naccess to the digital data will be limited to\nSIMBIOS Science Team members and other approved users as agreed upon by the SIMBIOS Project and\ndata providers.\nThe SIMBIOS Project will grant access to the international\nscience team members on a\ncase-by-case\nbasis according to ongoing collaboration\nagreements. Other investigators\nfrom the ocean color\ncommunity\nwill be able to query information\nabout the data (i.e., parameters,\nlocations,\ndates, and\ninvestigators),\nbut will not have access to the data itself. Instead, if they are interested in the data, they will\nbe referred to the provider. After the third year anniversary of data collection, the data will change from a\n"restricted"\nto an "open" status and will be distributed by National Oceanographic\nData Center (NODC).\nSome special data sets for algorithm development\nwill be made available\nto the research\ncommunity\nwithout restrictions\nwith the approval of the SIMBIOS Science Team.\nPrior to the three-year data collection anniversary,\nusers of data will be required to provide proper\ncredit and acknowledgment\nof the provider. A citation should also be made of the data archive. The\nprovider(s)\nshall have the right to be a named as a co-author. Users of data are encouraged\nto discuss\nrelevant findings with the provider early in the research. The user is required to give all providers of the\ndata being used a copy of any manuscript resulting from the use of the data prior to initial submission\nfor\npublication,\nthus providing the data provider an opportunity\nto comment\non the paper. All users and\nproviders\nare required to report possible data errors or mislabels found in the database to the SeaBASS\nadministration.\nA major purpose of the SeaBASS database is to facilitate comparisons\nbetween in situ observations\n(regionally, temporally,\nby technique, by investigator,\netc.), as well as between in situ and remotely sensed\nobservations.\nUpdates and corrections to submitted data sets are encouraged.\nRecords will be maintained\nof\nupdates and corrections and a summary of new and updated data will be posted online. It is the provider\'s\nresponsibility\nto ensure that the current data in the archive is identicai to the data used in the provider\'s\nmost recent publications\nor current research.\nAt the end of each SIMBIOS\ncontract,\na final data\nresubmission,\nor a written certification\nof data quality, from the provider is mandatory.\nAfter receiving the final data, the SIMBIOS Project will forward the data at the appropriate\ntime to\nNODC\nfor open distribution.\nA courtesy citation, naming the provider and the funding\nagency, will\naccompany the data. The SIMBIOS Project will not be held responsible for any data errors or misuse.\nTo afford continued\nrapid submission\nof data sets, the SeaBASS Web server is configured\npassword protected system. Additionally,\nthe Web server and SeaBASS software log all user activity.\ninformation\nis available to contributing\ninvestigators.\n\nas a\nThis\n\nREFERENCES\nFirestone, E.R., and S.B. I and A.2. Fargion\n\nLanham,\n\n3\n\nMaryland\n\nMaryland\nBeItsviIIe,\n\nMaryland\n\nFILE FORMAT\n\nThe format of a SeaBASS data file is straightforward: data are presented in columns (delimited\nby\nspaces, tabs, or commas) and are preceded by a series of predefmed metadata headers (Table B 1, see\nsucceeding\npages). Every header begins with a forward slash (/) and every data file opens with\n/begin_header.\nThe headers can then be listed in any order, so long as the list ends with/end_header.\nA value of NA ("not available" or "not applicable") is assigned to any header where information\ncannot be provided. Data files with missing headers will not be accepted for submission\nto SeaBASS.\nCommas separate multiple entries; white spaces and apostrophes\nare invalid entries. A list and description\nof the SeaBASS metadata headers is available online:\n<http://seabass.gsfc.nasa.gov/seabass_header.html>.\nThis list is updated regularly. Examples\nof the metadata\nheaders may be found in the example\nSeaBASS files located at the end of this chapter.\n\nFIELD\n\nNAMES\n\nAND UNITS\n\nIn an effort to ensure compatibility\nwithin the SeaBASS data archive, and to facilitate the development\nof the expanded version of the SeaBASS database, a standard set of case-insensitive\nfield names and units\nhas been adopted (Table B2). An online version of the standard field names list is available:\n<http:l/seabass.gsfc.nasa.govlcgi-binlstdfields.cgi>.\nWhile the current list of standardized field names is reasonably comprehensive,\nit cannot account for all of\nthe possible data types one might wish to provide to the SeaBASS archive. If a data type to be submitted to\nSeaBASS does not fall under one of the predefined standard field names, the investigator may still include\nthe data. Such non-standard data will be archived, but the geophysical data values will not be ingested into\nthe online database. The data will be retrievable, but only with the original archived file, not as a separate\ndata set. If there are frequent queries for non-standard data types, then the new field names and associated\nunits will be added to the online version of Table B2.\nTable B 1.
